Sa'y After Emancipation for Invalid Payment
Ruling on the slave's obligation to work for value after paying invalid substitute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave's obligation to work (sa'y) for the market value after paying an invalid substitute in kitaba, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the condition for emancipation.

Core Idea
Paying wine or pork in kitaba frees the slave, but he must work to pay the market value.

When a slave enters a kitaba contract with an invalid substitute (wine or pork), Hanafi law holds that emancipation still occurs upon payment. However, because the contract was fasid, the slave owes the master the market value of his person. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this ruling, the intra-Hanafi difference, and the distinction from a kitaba on a garment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? A master and slave agree on a kitaba with wine or pork as the payment. The slave pays those items. What is the hukm regarding emancipation and the slave's obligation afterwards?
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the text cite? It uses qiyas (analogy) to a fasid sale: if the sold item perishes, the buyer owes its value. Here, the slave is freed (like perishing), so he owes his value.
The dalil is from al-Hidayah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How exactly does the analogy prove the ruling? The slave's emancipation is equivalent to the perishing of the sold item; both make the original subject matter irrecoverable. In a fasid sale, the buyer must pay the value; similarly, the slave must pay his value.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa has an alternate narration: emancipation only occurs if the master explicitly conditions freedom on payment (e.g., 'If you pay them, you are free'). Otherwise, no emancipation. The zahir al-riwayah holds that emancipation occurs regardless of explicit condition.
The mufta bihi is the zahir al-riwayah position.
5
Distinguish from kitaba on a garment
Why is a garment different? Because the type of garment intended is uncertain; the slave cannot know which exact garment the master meant, so payment is not established. Wine and pork are specific substances, so payment is established.
This highlights that jahala (ignorance) in the subject matter can be removed by valuation, but not in the garment case because the intended item itself is ambiguous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسعى في قيمته إن أدى ما سمى، وفي ظاهر الرواية: إنما يثبت العتق والسعاية في القيمة إن أدى ما سمى، وهو الخمرُ والخِنزير، وعن أبي حنيفة له: أنه إنما يعتق بأداء عينهما إن قال: إن أدَّيْتَهُما فأنت حرّ، ولا فرق في ظاهر الرواية وإنما ثبت أداء القيمة إما بتصادقهما على ما أدى في قيمة العبد، أو بتقويم المقومين، وإذا اختلفوا في ذلك فلا يعتق ما لم يؤد أقصى القيمتين؛ لأنه المتيقن، بخلاف ما إذا كاتبه على ثوب حيث لا يعتق بأدائه؛ لأنه لا يتفطن على مراد العاقدة؛ لاختلاف إحساسه، فلا يثبت العتق بدون إرادته. لا يقال: إن قيمة العبد مجهولة لجهالة الثوب، فينبغي أن تؤثر تلك الجهالة في فساد العقد على وجه لا يعتق بأداء القيمة، كما لا يعتق بأداء الثوب؛ لأنا نقول: إن جهالة القيمة يمكن إزالتها بتقويم المقومين، فلا تأثير لها في فساد العقد، ولا في إبطاله ولا وقوف على أداء الشروط في الثوب، فاسم الثوب كما يتناول ما أدى يتناول غيره. ومعلوم أن مراده ليس مطلق الثوب؛ لأنه لا يزول ملكه عن العبد لأي ثوب كان، فكان المراد معيناً ولا يدري أن المؤدّي هل هو ذلك المعين أم لا، فلا يثبت الأداء. كذا فهم من تقرير «الكفاية». هكذا في «الجلبي). [١] قوله: وسعى في قيمة؛ أي إذا أعتق بأداء عين الخمر والخنزير لزمه أن يسعى في قيمته؛ لأنه وجب عليه أو رقبته لفساد العقد، وقد تعذر بالعتق، فيجب ردُّ قيمته كما في البيع الفاسد إذا تلف المبيع. كذا في «الهداية». [٢] قوله: ولا فرق ... الخ؛ أي لا فرق بين أن يقول: إن أديت الخمر أو الخنزير فأنت حر أو لم يقل. كذا في «العناية».
And he works for his value if he pays what was named. And in the apparent narration, emancipation and working for the value are established only if he pays what was named, i.e., wine and pig. And from Abu Hanifa [there is] that he is only emancipated by paying their actual substance if he says: 'If you pay them, you are free.' And there is no difference in the apparent narration; rather the payment of value is established either by their mutual agreement on what was paid in the value of the slave, or by the valuation of valuators. And if they differ on that, he is not emancipated until he pays the higher of the two values, because it is the certain. Contrary to if he made a kitaba on a garment, where he is not emancipated by paying it, because he does not pay attention to the intention of the contracting party due to the difference in its sensation, so emancipation is not established without his intention. It cannot be said that the value of the slave is unknown due to the ignorance of the garment, so that ignorance should affect the invalidity of the contract in a way that he is not emancipated by paying the value, just as he is not emancipated by paying the garment; for we say that the ignorance of the value can be removed by the valuation of valuators, so it has no effect on the invalidity of the contract, nor on its nullification. And there is no reliance on the performance of the condition in the garment, for the name 'garment' as it includes what was paid, includes other than it. And it is known that his intention is not an absolute garment, because his ownership does not leave the slave for any garment whatever, so the intended is specific and it is not known whether what is paid is that specific one or not, so the payment is not established. Thus understood from the exposition of al-Kifaya. This is in al-Jalabi. [1] His statement: 'and he works for the value'; i.e., if he is emancipated by paying the actual substance of wine and pig, it becomes necessary for him to work for its value, because it became due on him or on his neck due to the invalidity of the contract, and it became impossible [to return] due to emancipation, so his value must be returned, like in a fasid sale if the sold item perishes. This is in al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'and there is no difference ...'; i.e., there is no difference between him saying: 'If you pay the wine or pig, you are free' or not saying. This is in al-Inayah.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling (hukm) that after a mukatab is emancipated by paying wine or pork (the invalid substitute), he must work (sa'y) to pay his market value to the master. The text distinguishes between the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) and a narration from Abu Hanifa. In the apparent narration, emancipation and the obligation of sa'y both occur when the slave pays the named wine or pork. In Abu Hanifa's other narration, emancipation only occurs if the master explicitly conditioned freedom on payment of those substances; otherwise, he is not emancipated. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: why is a kitaba on a garment different? Because the garment is not a known substitute; its value can be estimated, but the garment itself is not deliverable as intended. Therefore, if the kitaba is on a garment, the slave is not emancipated by paying it. The author affirms that the value is determinable by mutual agreement or valuation, and if they disagree, the slave must pay the higher value to be certain. This ruling is analogous to a fasid sale where the sold item perishes: the buyer owes the value.

Key Terms
سعاية (saʿāyah): The slave's work to earn money to pay the value of his freedom after emancipation via a fasid kitaba.
تقويم المقومين (taqwīm al-muqawwimīn): Valuation by expert assessors to determine the market value of the slave.
بدل (badal): The substitute in kitaba; here, wine or pork is paid but considered invalid; the true substitute becomes the market value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because a garment is not valuable property for a Muslim.
B) Because the identity of the garment intended cannot be known with certainty.
C) Because the value of a garment is always unknown.
D) Because a garment cannot be owned by a slave.

Key Takeaway
A fasid kitaba still results in emancipation upon payment of the named invalid substitute, but the slave must then work (sa'y) to pay the market value of his person.

Mukatab Contracted for Wine or Pork: Value vs. Actual Payment
Examines the Hanafi dispute on paying wine/pork in kitaba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on a mukatab paying wine/pork and distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar, and apply the analogy to fasid sales.

Core Idea
In kitaba for wine or pork, freedom requires paying the value, not the physical items.

This passage examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: if a mukatab (slave under a manumission contract) is obligated to pay wine or pork (impermissible items), does he become free by delivering the actual items or by paying their monetary value? The Zahir al-Riwayah position, which is the most authoritative in the school, rules that freedom is achieved by paying the value. Imam Abu Hanifa himself held that freedom requires the actual items if explicitly conditioned. Understanding this dispute reveals how the Hanafi school handles fasid (defective) contracts through value substitution.

Guided Path
1
State the Zahir al-Riwayah position
Identify that in the most authoritative transmission, freedom and the obligation to earn (sa'ee) are only established if the slave pays the value (qimah) of the wine or pork, not the physical items themselves.
This is the mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) position.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's opposing view
Imam Abu Hanifa held that the slave becomes free only by paying the exact items (ayn) if the contract explicitly said 'if you pay them, you are free.' This literal interpretation bases freedom on fulfillment of the specific condition.
This view is not relied upon in practice.
3
Understand the analogy to fasid sale
The commentary draws a parallel: if a fasid sale involves an impermissible item that perishes, the buyer must pay its value. Similarly, the kitaba contract is fasid because the consideration (wine/pork) is invalid, so value substitutes.
This is a qiyas jali (clear analogy).
4
Examine the dispute on resolving unknown values
If the master and slave disagree on the value of the slave at the time of contract, the slave must pay the higher of the two values to be certain. This differs from a kitaba on a garment, where the intended garment is unknown and payment cannot be established.
The uncertainty in value can be removed by expert valuation, unlike uncertainty in the object itself.
5
Distinguish the three intra-Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa: freedom only by actual items if explicitly conditioned. Abu Yusuf: freedom by either actual items or value. Zufar: freedom only by value. The Zahir al-Riwayah aligns with Zufar's view (value only), and this is the relied-upon position.
The text notes Zufar's reasoning that the intention is payment of the value of the slave's self, not the wine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المكاتب تصرفاته وعجزه وأحكامه
وسعى في قيمته إن أدى ما سمى
وسعى في قيمته إن أدى ما سمّى ، وفي ظاهر الرواية : إنما يثبت العتق والسعاية في القيمة إن أدى ما سمى، وهو الخمرُ والخِنزير، وعن أبي حنيفة له : أنه إنما يعتق بأداء عينهما إن قال : إن أدَّيْتَهُما فأنت حرّ، ولا فرق في ظاهر الرواية وإنما ثبت أداء القيمة إما بتصادقهما على ما أدى في قيمة العبد، أو بتقويم المقومين، وإذا اختلفوا في ذلك فلا يعتق ما لم يؤد أقصى القيمتين ؛ لأنه المتيقن ، بخلاف ما إذا كـاتـب عـلـى ثـوب حيث لا يعتق بأدائه ؛ لأنه لا يتفطن على مرادِ العاقبة ؛ لاختلافِ إحساسه، فلا يثبت العتق بدون إرادته.
لا يقال : إن قيمة العبدِ مجهولة لجهالة الثوب، فينبغي أن تؤثر تلك الجهالة في فساد العقدِ على وجه لا يعتق بأداء القيمة، كما لا يعتقُ بأداء الثوب ؛ لأنا نقول : إنّ جهالة القيمة يمكن إزالتها بتقويم المقومين، فلا تأثير لها في فساد العقد، ولا في إبطاله
ولا وقوف على أداء الشروط في الثوب ، فاسم الثوب كما يتناولُ ما أدى يتناولُ غيره. ومعلوم أن مراده ليس مطلق الثوب ؛ لأنه لا يزول ملكه عن العبد لأي ثوب كان، فكان المراد معيناً ولا يدري أنّ المؤدّي هل هو ذلك المعين أم لا ، فلا يثبت الأداء. كذا فهم من تقرير «الكفاية». هكذا في «الجلبي).
[١] قوله : وسعى في قيمة ؛ أي إذا أعتق بأداء عين الخمر والخنزير لزمه أن يسعى في قيمته ؛ لأنه وجب عليه أو رقبته لفساد العقد، وقد تعذر بالعتق ، فيجب ردُّ قيمته كما في البيع الفاسد إذا تلف المبيع. كذا في «الهداية»(۳).
[٢] قوله : ولا فرق ... الخ ؛ أي لا فرق بين أن يقول : إن أديت الخمر أو الخنزير فأنت حر أو لم يقل. كذا في «العناية»(4).
[١] قوله : إن أدى العين عتق ؛ لأنه بدل صورة، وإن أدّى القيمة عتق أيضاً ؛ لأنها البدلُ معنى ؛ لأنَّ المراد أداءً قيمة نفس العبد على ما نقله الأكمل، وإن كان ظاهر دلیل زفرَ الله يشعر بكون المراد بهما قيمة الخمر. كذا في الجلبي)
Book of the Mukatab: his transactions, incapacity, and rulings. And he strives for its value if he pays what was named. And in Zahir al-Riwayah: freedom and earning the value are only established if he pays what was named, which is wine and pork. And from Abu Hanifa: he only becomes free by paying the identical items if he said: 'If you pay them, you are free.' There is no difference in Zahir al-Riwayah. Payment of value is established either by mutual agreement on what was paid in the value of the slave, or by valuation by valuators. If they differ on that, he does not become free until he pays the higher of the two values, because that is certain. Unlike if he makes a kitaba on a garment, where he does not become free by paying it, because he cannot ascertain the intention of the contract due to different perceptions, so freedom is not established without his intention. It cannot be said: The value of the slave is unknown due to the unknown nature of the garment, so that unknown should affect the invalidity of the contract such that he does not become free by paying the value, just as he does not become free by paying the garment; because we say: The unknown of the value can be removed by valuation by valuators, so it has no effect on the invalidity of the contract, nor on its nullification. And there is no reliance on fulfilling the conditions regarding the garment, because the term 'garment' encompasses what he paid as well as others. It is known that his intention is not the absolute garment, because his ownership over the slave does not cease for any garment, so the intended item is specific, and it is not known whether the paid item is that specific one or not, so payment is not established. Thus understood from the exposition of Al-Kifayah. Thus in Al-Jalabi. [1] His statement: 'And he strives for its value' means that if he is freed by paying the identical wine and pork, he must strive for its value, because it became due on him or on his neck due to the invalidity of the contract, and it became impossible due to freedom, so its value must be returned, as in a fasid sale if the sold item perishes. Thus in Al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'And there is no difference...' means there is no difference between saying 'If you pay the wine or pork you are free' or not saying it. Thus in Al-Inayah. [1] His statement: 'If he pays the identical item, he becomes free, because it is a substitute in form; and if he pays the value, he also becomes free, because it is a substitute in meaning, because the intention is payment of the value of the slave's self as Al-Akmal transmitted, although Zufar's apparent proof suggests that what is intended by both is the value of the wine. Thus in Al-Jalabi.)
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage deals with the legal ruling of a mukatab (slave contracted for manumission) who is required to pay wine or pork (khamr wa khinzir) as part of the kitaba contract. The passage first presents the Zahir al-Riwayah position, which is the most authoritative in the Hanafi school: the slave becomes free and must earn the value of these items if he pays what was named (i.e., the wine or pork). Freedom is established only by paying the value, not the physical items themselves. Then it reports a different view from Imam Abu Hanifa: he becomes free only by paying the actual wine or pork, and only if the contract explicitly said 'if you pay them you are free.' However, according to Zahir al-Riwayah, there is no difference in this condition—freedom is by value either way. The passage explains that payment of value is established either by mutual agreement between master and slave on the value, or by valuation of experts. If they disagree, the slave must pay the higher of the two values to be certain. This is contrasted with a kitaba on a garment (thawb): if the slave pays a garment, he does not become free because the intended garment is unknown—it could be any garment. The reason is that the master's ownership does not cease for just any garment, so the specific garment intended is unknown. The passage then addresses an objection: if the value of the slave is unknown due to the unknown garment, why doesn't that cause the contract to be invalid, preventing freedom by value? The answer is that the unknown value can be removed by expert valuation, so it does not invalidate the contract. Finally, the footnotes elaborate: footnote [1] explains that if the slave is freed by paying the actual items, he must strive to pay the value because the contract was invalid and the slave's neck was in pledge; after freedom, the value is due as in a fasid sale. Footnote [2] confirms that there is no difference in the ruling whether the contract explicitly mentions 'if you pay...' or not. The footnote from page 10 (footnote [1]) presents the views of Abu Yusuf and Zufar: Abu Yusuf says he becomes free whether he pays the actual items or the value, while Zufar says he only becomes free by paying the value. The Zahir al-Riwayah aligns with the view that freedom is by paying the value.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract (kitaba) with his master to be freed upon paying a specified sum or delivering specified items.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The most authoritative transmitted rulings in the Hanafi school, from the six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
قيمة (qimah): The monetary value or compensation due instead of the physical item in a fasid contract.
سعى (sa'ee): The obligation of the mukatab to earn money to pay the value; he must strive to work and pay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling regarding a mukatab who pays the value (instead of the physical items) of wine or pork he was contracted to pay?
A) He does not become free because he did not pay the exact items promised.
B) He becomes free only if the contract did not explicitly mention 'pay these items'.
C) He becomes free, and the payment of value is valid because the items are invalid for consideration.
D) He becomes free only if he pays both the items and the value.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that a mukatab must pay the value of impermissible items like wine or pork; the contract is fasid and value substitutes for the invalid consideration.

Value in a Fasid Kitaba: No Decrease Below Named Amount
States the ruling that the value due in an invalid kitaba cannot be less than the named amount.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling on the value due in a fasid kitaba and apply the analogy to fasid sale to determine the payable amount.

Core Idea
In a fasid kitaba, the value due is at least the named amount, never less.

The rule for a valid kitaba is clear: the slave pays the agreed price. But what happens when the kitaba is invalid? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a subtle point: the slave must pay the value of his services, but with a floor—the named amount cannot be undercut. The reasoning is drawn by analogy to a fasid sale, yet with a unique consideration for the slave's freedom. Let's unpack the proof and see why the floor exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
State the ruling: in a fasid kitaba, the value due is not decreased below the named amount and is increased above it. The condition is that the value is from the same genus as the named amount.
2
Locate the proof: qiyas on fasid sale
The proof is analogical reasoning (qiyas). The asl is a fasid sale where the sold item perishes; the buyer must pay the value (high or low). The far' is the fasid kitaba; the illa is that the contract is invalid, so value substitutes the agreed countervalue.
3
Analyze the special element in kitaba
Unlike fasid sale, in kitaba the slave's freedom is at stake. The master's consent to the named amount and the slave's willingness to pay more to secure freedom create a floor. The ruling ensures the slave's right to freedom is not nullified.
Text from Al-Hidayah: 'the master is satisfied with a decrease, and the slave is satisfied with an increase'.
4
Apply to the given scenario
Apply the ruling: The named amount is 1000, value is 800 → pay 1000; value is 1200 → pay 1200.
Values from same genus (currency).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يُنْقَص مما سمّي وزيد عليه، هذه مسألة مبتدأة لا تعلق لها بمسألة الخمر والخنزير، ومعناها : أن القيمة في الكتابة الفاسدة إذا كانـت مـن جـنس المسمى، فإن كانت ناقصةً عن المسمَّى لا تنقص عن المسمى، وإن كانت زائدة زيدت عليه ، ووضع المسألة في «المبسوط» فيما إذا كاتب عبده بألف على أن يخدمه أبداً، فالكتابة فاسدة، فتجب القيمة ، فإن كانت ناقصة . عن الألف لا تنقص عنه ، وإن كانت زائدة زيدت عليه.
[٢] قوله : زيدت عليه ؛ لأنه عقد فاسد ، فيجب القيمة عند هلاك المبدل بالغة ما بلغت ، كما في البيع الفاسد ؛ وهذا لأنَّ المولى راض بالنقصان، والعبد راض بالزيادة، كيلا يبطل حقه في العتق أصلاً ، فتجب القيمة بالغاً ما بلغت. كذا في «الهداية»(۲).
And it is not decreased from what was named and is increased upon it. This is a new issue unrelated to the issue of wine and pork. Its meaning is that the value in an invalid kitaba, if it is of the same genus as the named amount, if it is less than the named amount, it does not decrease from it, and if it is more, it is increased upon it. The scenario in Al-Mabsut is when a man wrote a kitaba with his slave for a thousand on the condition that he serve him forever; the kitaba is invalid, so the value is due. If it is less than a thousand, it does not decrease from it, and if it is more, it is increased upon it. [2] His statement: 'Increased upon it' because it is an invalid contract, so the value is due upon destruction of the substitute, whatever it reaches, as in a fasid sale. This is because the master is satisfied with a decrease, and the slave is satisfied with an increase, so that his right to freedom is not nullified at all, so the value is due whatever it reaches. Thus in Al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage introduces a distinct mas'ala (issue) regarding the value due in a fasid (invalid) kitaba. The ruling is that if the value is from the same genus as the specified amount, it must at least equal that amount; if it is higher, it is increased. The scenario is given from Al-Mabsut: a man writes a kitaba with his slave for 1000 on condition of perpetual service; this kitaba is fasid. Upon invalidation, the slave must pay the value of his services. If the value assessed is less than 1000, the slave must still pay 1000 (the named amount). If the value is more, he pays the higher amount. The footnote explains the reasoning: because it is a fasid contract, the value is due upon destruction of the substitute, similar to a fasid sale. The master is content with a lower amount (since he specified 1000), and the slave is content with a higher amount (to secure his freedom), so the value is due in full no matter what.

Key Terms
كتابة فاسدة (kitaba fasida): An invalid contract of manumission due to a defect such as an impossible condition (e.g., perpetual service).
المسمى (al-musamma): The named amount or item specified in the kitaba contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid kitaba where the named amount is 1000, if the value is 800, what must be paid?
A) 800
B) 1000
C) 0
D) 1200

Key Takeaway
The value in a fasid kitaba is never less than the named amount but can be more, analogized from fasid sale.

Ruling on Kitaba for an Animal with Only Genus Specified
Clarifies the ruling when the kitaba is on an animal with only the genus mentioned.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a kitaba contract is valid even if only the animal's genus is specified, and explain the qiyas to the case of marriage dower.

Core Idea
Kitaba for an unspecified animal is valid; the slave pays the average or its value.

This lesson examines a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding kitaba contracts where the payment is an animal described only by its genus (jins). At first glance, such uncertainty might seem to render the contract invalid due to gharar. However, Hanafi law validates it by analogy (qiyas) to the marriage dower (mahr), where the same situation is permitted. Understanding this ruling illustrates the Hanafi approach to mitigating uncertainty through the concept of 'wasat' (the average).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a kitaba contract with payment of an animal specified only by genus. The ruling is that it is valid (sahih).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to the case of mahr (dower) where only the genus is specified.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illa) is that in both cases, the unspecified item can be determined by reference to the average (wasat), avoiding uncertainty.
4
State the practical outcome
The slave must pay the average animal of that genus, or its value; the master can be forced to accept the value.
5
Note the absence of ikhtilaf
The text indicates no internal Hanafi dispute; this is the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصحت على حيوان ذَكَرَ جنسه فقط) : أي لم يذكر نوعَهُ وصفته : ( ويؤدي الوسط، أو قيمته) () ، إنما يخير ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ أصل من وجه،
[٣]قوله : ويؤدي الوسط أو قيمته ؛ ويجبر على قبول القيمة ؛ لأنه قضاء في معنى الأداء، كما إذا تزوج المرأة على حيوان، وبين جنسه ولم يبين نوعه وصفته، فإنّه يجوز، وينصرفُ إلى الوسط، ويجبر على قبول القيمة. هكذا في العيني).
And it is valid on an animal for which he mentioned only its genus: i.e., he did not mention its type and description. And he pays the average, or its value. He is given a choice because each is a root in some aspect. [3] His statement: 'And he pays the average or its value' and he is forced to accept the value, because it is a discharge in the meaning of performance, just as if a man marries a woman on an animal, and he specifies its genus but does not specify its type and description, it is valid, and it is referred to the average, and he is forced to accept the value. Thus in Al-Ayni.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage deals with a valid kitaba where the slave promises to pay an animal, but only the genus is mentioned (e.g., 'a camel' without specifying what type or quality). The ruling is that the contract is valid, and the slave must pay the average (medium) animal of that genus, or its value. The master can be compelled to accept the value. The reason is that the average is a fair compromise, and accepting the value is a valid settlement. The analogy is given to a marriage dower (mahr): if a man marries a woman with a dower of an animal specified only by genus, it is valid, and he must pay the average, or she can be forced to accept the value.

Key Terms
وسط (wasat): The average or medium quality of the genus, not the best or worst.
جنس (jins): The broad category of an item (e.g., animal, camel) without specifying sub-type.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Any animal
B) The best animal
C) The average animal or its value
D) The worst animal

Key Takeaway
A kitaba contract for an animal specified only by genus is valid, and the slave must pay the average animal or its value, following the same principle as a marriage dower.

Kitaba on an Unspecified Animal: The Intra-Hanafi Payment Dispute
Explaining the validity of kitaba on an unspecified animal and the intra-Hanafi dispute on payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on kitaba payment and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Zufar regarding the mode of payment.

Core Idea
In kitaba on an animal of unspecified type, paying either the medium animal or its value frees the slave per the majority, but Zufar demands value only.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the mode of payment in a kitaba contract for an animal described only by its genus (e.g., 'a horse'). While all Hanafi jurists agree the contract itself is valid, they diverge on whether the slave must deliver the actual animal or may substitute its monetary value. The majority view, held by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, grants the slave a choice, whereas Zufar insists on value payment alone, drawing an analogy to the prohibition of wine. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish between formal and meaningful substitutes in contractual obligations.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its conditions
Identify that the kitaba contract is valid when the animal is described only by genus. The slave must deliver a medium-quality animal (al-wasat) or its value. Determine the condition: specification of genus suffices; full description is not required.
All parties agree on the validity of the contract; the dispute is solely about the payment mode.
2
Identify the parties and their positions
Recognize Abu Hanifa (and Abu Yusuf) holding that both the 'ayn (medium animal) and the value lead to freedom. Contrast with Zufar, who requires value only.
Abu Yusuf is explicitly mentioned in the text, but Abu Hanifa shares this view per later commentaries.
3
Analyse Zufar's proof
Examine Zufar's analogy to wine: just as a Muslim is prohibited from wine and must pay its value, here the animal is permissible but the value must be paid. Identify the operative analogy and its basis in the text.
Zufar extends the rule from a prohibited substance to a permissible one, arguing that value substitution is a general principle.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand the majority's reply: the prohibition on wine is specific (khas), not general. Animals are permissible property, so paying the actual animal is valid. The 'ayn is a formal substitute (badal sura), the value is a meaningful substitute (badal ma'na).
This response highlights the distinction between formal and meaningful substitutes in Hanafi legal theory.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical consequence (thamarah): if the slave pays the animal, Zufar would not free him, requiring an additional payment. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as affirmed by later authoritative texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصحت على حيوان ذَكَرَ جنسه فقط، ويؤدي الوسط أو قيمته. وعند أبي يوسف له إن أدى العين عتق، وإن أدى القيمة عتق أيضاً. وعند زفر لا يعتق إلا بأداء القيمة؛ لأن المسلم نهي عن اقتراب الخمر، فاقيمت القيمة مقامها.
And it is valid on an animal for which he mentioned only its genus, and he pays the medium or its value. According to Abu Yusuf, if he pays the 'ayn (the animal itself), he is freed; if he pays the value, he is also freed. According to Zufar, he is not freed except by paying the value, because the Muslim is prohibited from approaching wine, so the value is made to stand in its place.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of kitaba on an animal described only by genus (e.g., 'a horse' without specifying type or quality). The contract is valid (sahih), and the slave has a choice to pay the medium animal (al-wasat) or its monetary value. Abu Yusuf holds that either form of payment results in freedom. Zufar (Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl) argues that only payment of value suffices, drawing an analogy to the earlier prohibition of wine: a Muslim cannot own or deliver wine, so value substitutes. The majority position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is that both are valid because each is a substitute from a different perspective: the animal itself is a formal substitute, the value is a meaningful substitute. The mufta bihi position is that paying either the medium animal or its value leads to freedom.

Key Terms
الوسط (al-wasat): The medium or average animal of the genus mentioned, neither the best nor the worst.
بدل صورة (badal sura): A substitute that mirrors the form of the original obligation, here the animal itself.
قيمة (qima): Monetary value, especially as a substitute for a specified commodity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa, what modes of payment free the slave in a kitaba on an animal described only by genus?
A) Only the medium animal
B) Only the value of the medium animal
C) Either the medium animal or its value
D) Neither; the contract is invalid

Key Takeaway
In a Hanafi kitaba contract for an animal specified only by genus, the slave may pay either the medium animal or its value, and both lead to freedom per the majority view, which is the mufta bihi.

Wala' of a Mukatab Writing Another Mukatab
Determining the wala' when one mukatab writes another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mukatab writing a kitabah for another slave, explain the role of istihsan in overriding qiyas, and determine who gets the wala' based on the timing of payments.

Core Idea
A mukatab can write a kitabah for another slave (istihsan) and wala' goes to him if the second pays after his freedom; otherwise to the master.

This passage from 'Husn al-Dirayah' addresses a nuanced issue: a mukatab himself writing a kitabah for another slave. At first glance, qiyas would prohibit it, but the Hanafi school employs istihsan to permit it as a means of acquiring money. The real intricacy lies in determining who gains the wala' — the first mukatab or the original master — depending on the sequence of payments and freedom. Understanding this ruling clarifies the special capacity of the mukatab in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qiyas ruling and its basis
State that qiyas would deem the kitabah contract invalid because the mukatab is himself not fully qualified to effect manumission. This is the initial analogical ruling.
The qiyas considers the consequence (manumission) and the capacity of the contracting party.
2
Recognize the istihsan exception
Explain that istihsan permits the contract because it is a means of acquiring money, and the manumission is ultimately attributed to the master. The mukatab's contract is valid but his manumission authority is limited.
Istihsan overrides strict analogy due to practical benefit.
3
Analyze the wala' scenarios
There are two main scenarios: if the second slave pays after the first mukatab is freed, the first mukatab gets the wala'; if the second pays before the first is freed, the master gets the wala'. The reasoning is based on who is the direct agent of manumission and who has a right of ownership.
The wala' follows the actual emancipator — the one who causes freedom.
4
Apply to additional cases
If both pay together, wala' goes to the master (original owner). If the first fails to pay and is returned to slavery, the second's wala' remains with the master. The commentary from al-Hidayah and al-Ghurar provides these details.
The master's original right prevails when the first mukatab's role is incomplete.
5
Summarize the overall position
The contract is valid (sahih) via istihsan, and wala' determination is based on a clear set of conditions. This shows how Hanafi fiqh balances qiyas with istihsan to achieve equitable outcomes.
This ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah and considered agreed upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية
وله ولاؤه إن أدى بعد عتقه، ولسيده إن أدى قبله لا تزوجه إلا بإذنه ولا هبته لا يجوز الكتابة وهو القياس؛ لأنها تؤدي إلى العتق، وهو ليس من أهله، وجه الاستحسان : أنها إفادة المال، وعتقهُ يضاف إلى المولى، وله ولاؤه إن أدى عتقه، ولسيده إن أدى قبله ) : أي للمكاتب الأوَّلِ ولاءُ الثَّانِي إِن أَدَّى الثاني بعد عتق الأول، ولسيده إن أدى قبله ، لا تزوجه إلا بإذنه ولا هبته
[0]
بعد
[١]قوله : وجه الاستحسان... الخ، توضيحه : إنّ كلَّ واحدٍ منهما عقد اكتساب المال فيملكه كالبيع، وقد يكون عقد الكتابة أنفع له من البيع ؛ لأنه لا يزيل الملك إلا بعد وصول البدل إليه ، والبيع يزيله قبله ؛ ولهذا يملكه الأب والوصي. [٢] قوله : إن أدّى بعد عتقه ؛ لأنَّ العاقد ؛ أي المكاتب الأول من أهل ثبوت
الولاء، وهو الأصل ؛ لأنه مباشر للعتق ، فيثبت الولاء له. [٣] قوله : ولسيده إن أدّى قبله ؛ لأنّ له فيه نوع ملك، ويصح إضافة الإعتاق إليه في الجملة، فإذا تعذر إضافته إلى مباشر العقد وهو المكاتب الأوّل ؛ لعدم الأهلية أضيف إليه، كما في العبد المأذون إذا اشترى شيئاً يثبت الملك للمولى ، فكذا هذا. هكذا في «الهداية»(۱). [٤] قوله : إن أدى الثاني بعد عتق الأول... الخ ؛ أما إن أديا بدلهما جميعاً معاً فولاؤهما للمولى ترجيحاً للأصل، وإن عجز الأول عن الأداء وردّ إلى الرق، ولم يؤدّ الثاني بدله بقي مكاتباً، فإن أدّى البدل إلى المولى عتق، وإن عجز ردّ إلى الرق كالأول. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» (۲) ناقلاً . عن «الغرر»(۳).
Husn al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah. And he has his wala' if he pays after his manumission, and his master has it if he pays before it. He may not marry except with his permission, nor give gifts. The kitabah is not permissible, and this is the qiyas, because it leads to manumission, and he is not qualified for it. The basis of istihsan is that it is a means of acquiring money, and his manumission is attributed to the master. And he has his wala' if he pays his manumission, and his master has it if he pays before it. That is, for the first mukatab is the wala' of the second if the second pays after the first's manumission, and for his master if he pays before it. He may not marry except with his permission, nor give gifts. [0] After. [1] His statement: The basis of istihsan... etc. Its explanation: Each of them is a contract for acquiring money, so he owns it like sale, and the kitabah contract may be more beneficial to him than sale because it does not remove ownership until the consideration reaches him, while sale removes it before. For this reason, the father and guardian own it. [2] His statement: If he pays after his manumission, because the contracting party, that is the first mukatab, is qualified for the establishment of wala', and it is the principle, because he is the direct agent of manumission, so wala' is established for him. [3] His statement: And for his master if he pays before it, because he has a kind of ownership in him, and it is valid to attribute the manumission to him in a general sense. So if it is impossible to attribute it to the direct agent of the contract, which is the first mukatab, due to lack of qualification, it is attributed to him, just as in the case of a permitted slave if he buys something, ownership is established for the master, and similarly here. This is in al-Hidayah (1). [4] His statement: If the second pays after the first's manumission... etc. If both pay their considerations together, then their wala' is for the master, giving precedence to the original. If the first is unable to pay and is returned to slavery, and the second does not pay his consideration, he remains a mukatab. Then if he pays the consideration to the master, he is freed, and if he is unable, he is returned to slavery like the first. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi (2) quoting from al-Ghurar (3).
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage discusses the wala' (clientage) when a mukatab writes a kitabah for another slave. The ruling is that the first mukatab (the one who writes) has the wala' of the second if the second pays his kitabah after the first has been freed. If the second pays before the first is freed, the wala' goes to the master. The commentary explains that the first approach (qiyas) would not allow the first mukatab to write a kitabah because it leads to manumission and he is not legally capable of that. However, istihsan permits it because it is a means of acquiring money, and the manumission is attributed to the master. The details of wala' timing and cases where both pay together or one fails are also given.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-wala'): Clientage; the relationship of inheritance and loyalty that results from freeing a slave.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has a contract of kitabah for his freedom upon payment.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here it is the initial analogical ruling that is overridden by istihsan.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that favors a ruling based on convenience or equity over strict analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a mukatab writes a kitabah for another slave, and the second slave pays his kitabah after the first mukatab has been freed, who gets the wala'?
A) The original master
B) The first mukatab
C) The second slave gains his own wala'
D) The wala' is split equally

Key Takeaway
A mukatab may validly write a kitabah for another slave via istihsan, and the wala' goes to the first mukatab if the second pays after his freedom, otherwise to the master.

Authority of Father and Executor Over Minor's Slaves: Comparison with the Mukatab
Comparison of authority over minor's slaves with mukatab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which transactions over a minor's slaves are valid for the father and executor and identify why a ma'dhun, mudarib, or sharik cannot perform them.

Core Idea
The father and executor may write kitabah for the minor's slave but cannot manumit him for a price — just like a mukatab's authority over his own slave.

This passage clarifies the scope of authority that a father and an executor (wasi) have over the slaves belonging to a minor. The Hanafi school analogizes their position to that of a mukatab — a slave under a contract of manumission. Just as a mukatab can engage in transactions that bring profit to himself but cannot waste the slave's value, so too the father/executor can only perform acts that bring wealth to the minor. The commentary then excludes other agents — the ma'dhun (permitted slave), mudarib, and sharik — from having any such powers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups compared
The passage states that the father and executor regarding the minor's slaves are like the mukatab regarding his own slave. Note the analogy: the father/executor stand in the same position as the mukatab, but only in certain transactions.
The mukatab is a slave who has a contract to earn his freedom; he can own earnings but not give away the slave.
2
Define the scope of the analogy
The father/executor may do any transaction that brings wealth to the minor, similar to how the mukatab acquires wealth. This includes writing kitabah (a contract of manumission for a price) because that brings in money, but excludes manumission for a price (which is 'above' kitabah and voids the slave's value) and selling the slave to himself (self-dealing, a waste).
Key phrase: 'فإنَّهما يملكان تصرفا يحصلُ به المالُ للصَّغير' - they own a transaction that brings wealth to the minor.
3
Apply the analogy to derive specific rulings
Thus the father/executor may perform kitabah of the minor's slave, but not manumission for a price, nor sale of the slave to himself. These would be wasteful and are therefore invalid.
Kitabah is valid because it brings payment; manumission for a price is invalid because it is 'above' kitabah - it effectively gives freedom for a lump sum but wastes potential future earnings.
4
Exclude other actors: ma'dhun, mudarib, and sharik
The passage explicitly states that none of the prohibited acts (like kitabah, manumission, etc.) are valid from a ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade), a mudarib (silent partner), or a sharik (partner). Even though they may engage in trade, they lack authority over the slave's person in these ways.
The ma'dhun's permission is limited to trade; he cannot marry his owner's slaves or write kitabah. The mudarib and sharik are similarly restricted to the scope of their contract.
5
Resolve textual ambiguity about 'marrying his female slave'
The commentary clarifies that when the base text said 'And none of this is valid from the ma'dhun…', it referred only to a specific part of the list (starting from 'do not marry him'), not to the earlier mention of marrying his female slave, because that act was conjuncted to sale and purchase, which are permissible for the ma'dhun. Thus the ma'dhun cannot marry his owner's female slave either, but not because of this particular passage.
This step shows the commentator's method of resolving apparent contradictions by analyzing the syntactic structure (conjunction).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو بمال)؛ لأنه فوق الكتابة، وبيع نفس عبده منه، وإنكاحه)، فإنَّ ذلك إعتاق، وهذا إتلاف مال. والأب والوصي في رقيق الصغير كالمكاتب): أي كل تصرف يملكُهُ المكاتب في عبده يملكانه في رقيق الصغير، وما لا فلا ، فإنَّهما يملكان تصرفا يحصلُ به المالُ للصَّغير كالمكاتب يملك كسب المال، فحكمهما حكمه، فيملكان كتابة عبده لا إعتاقه على مال، وبيع عبده من نفسه. (And also with money) because it is above kitabah. And the sale of his own slave to himself, and his marriage [of his female slave] – for that is manumission, and this is destruction of wealth. And the father and the executor regarding the minor's slaves are like the mukatab: meaning every transaction that the mukatab has over his own slave, they have over the minor's slaves, and what not. For they have a transactional act that brings wealth to the minor, just as the mukatab owns the acquisition of wealth, so their ruling is his ruling. Thus they may have the kitabah of his slave, but not his manumission for a price, nor the sale of his slave to himself. (And none of this is valid from the ma'dhun, mudarib, and sharik): meaning from his statement: 'do not marry him' up to here. As for marrying his female slave and having kitabah of his slave, these are not permissible for the ma'dhun, but he did not include them in his statement 'and none of this'; rather he mentioned them in the Book of al-Ma'dhun with his statement: 'And he does not marry his slaves nor his mukatab'. Because his statement here: 'and marrying his female slave' is conjuncted to sale and purchase, and both are permissible for the ma'dhun, so specifying the reference in his statement 'and none of this' to some of the conjuncted items without others is not good; so he made the reference to his statement: 'do not marry him' to the end.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is part of the commentary on the ruling that a mukatab may transact with his slave for money (like buying himself) but this is above his kitabah contract. The commentator explains that a father and an executor (over a minor) are similar to a mukatab in terms of their authority over the minor's slaves: they can do transactions that bring benefit, like writing a kitabah contract, but they cannot manumit for a price or sell the slave to himself because that would be wasting wealth. The passage then clarifies that the ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade), mudarib (silent partner), and sharik (partner) do not have these powers. The commentator addresses a potential confusion: the text mentions 'marrying his female slave' which is not permissible for the ma'dhun, but it is not included in the phrase 'none of this is valid' because it is conjuncted to sale and purchase, which the ma'dhun can do. Thus the reference is limited to the part from 'do not marry him' onward.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has a contract of manumission; can earn and transact within limits.
مأذون (ma'dhūn): A slave who is given permission by his master to engage in trade, but his transactions are limited.
مضارب (muḍārib): A person who enters into a mudaraba (silent partnership) contract; he acts as an agent but has limited authority.
شريك (sharīk): A partner in a business (especially shirkat al-'aqd); his authority is joint.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The mudarib
C) The father of the minor

Key Takeaway
The father and executor have the same authority over a minor's slaves as a mukatab has over his own: they may write kitabah but not manumit for a price or sell to themselves.

Who Enters the Mukatab's Contract by Purchase
Ruling on inclusion of child and parents in kitabah by purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on which relatives enter a mukatab's kitabah by purchase and explain the legal basis (kasb vs milk) for the restriction.

Core Idea
A mukatab's purchase includes only his child and parents—others do not enter because he has kasb, not milk.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: which relatives of a mukatab enter his kitabah if he purchases them? The matn of al-Wiqayah gives Abu Hanifa's view that only direct lineage (child and parents) enter. The Sahibayn extend this to all mahram relatives. Understanding the basis—the mukatab's limited ownership (kasb vs milk)—is crucial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the Ikhtilaf
State Abu Hanifa's position (only child and parents enter) and the Sahibayn's position (any mahram relative enters, like brother or uncle).
2
Analyze the proof of Abu Hanifa
Examine his talil: the mukatab has kasb (earning) not milk (ownership). Maintenance (nafaqah) is obligatory only for direct lineage when one has earnings, but for other relatives wealth (yasar) is required, which the mukatab lacks. Hence, only those with direct lineage enter.
The mukatab's earning capacity is sufficient only for lineal relatives.
3
Analyze the proof of the Sahibayn
They apply qiyas: if a free person purchases a mahram relative, that relative becomes free. Similarly, the mukatab's purchase should cause entry into kitabah for all mahram relatives, as a means of gradual emancipation.
They emphasize the similarity to freeing (itaq) in effect.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: If a mukatab purchases his brother, according to Abu Hanifa the brother does not enter kitabah and remains a slave; according to the Sahibayn, the brother enters kitabah and shares the payment obligation.
This affects the brother's status and payment schedule.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi works prefer the view of the Sahibayn as it is more comprehensive and practical. Thus, the operative ruling is that any mahram relative enters the kitabah when purchased.
Tarjih is based on practice (tamal) and need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويكاتب عليه بالشراء ولده وأبواه، لا من لا ولاد بينهما. هذا عند أبي حنيفة له، وعندهما: إن اشترى ذا رحم محرم كالأخ والعم يدخل في كتابته، كما يعتق عليه. له: إن للمكاتب كسباً لا ملكاً، فجعل الكسب كافياً للصلة في قرابة الولادة إذ القادر على الكسب مخاطب بالنفقة في الولادة لا في غيره، إذ لا بد فيه من اليسار. ثم قوله: ويكاتب عليه؛ أي يصير مكاتباً مثله. قوله: ولده وأبواه؛ لأنه من أهل أن يكاتب، وإن لم يكن من أهل الإعتاق فيجعل مكاتباً تحقيقاً لصلة الرحم بقدر الإمكان. كذا في «النهاية». قوله: لا من ولاد... الخ؛ أي لا يدخل في كتابته من لا ولاد بينهما. قوله: كما يعتق عليه؛ أي إذا كان المكاتب حراً واشترى الأخ أو العم، يعتق كل واحد منهما عليه. قوله: لا ملكاً؛ ولهذا يحل الصدقة له، وإن أصاب مالاً ولا يملك الهبة، ولا يفسد نكاح امرأته إذا اشتراها. قوله: كافياً للصلة؛ أراد أن قدرته على الكسب توجب الصلة في قرابة الولاد. كذا في بعض حواشي «الهداية». وأقواهم دخولاً الولد المولود في كتابته ثم الولد المشترى ثم الأبوان؛ ولهذا يتفاوتون في الأحكام، فإن المولود في كتابته يكون حكمه كحكم أبيه، حتى إذا مات أبوه ولم يترك وفاء سعى على نجوم أبيه، والولد المشترى يؤدّي بدل الكتابة حالاً وإلا ردّ إلى الرق، والوالدان يردان إلى الرق ولا يؤديان حالاً ولا مؤجّلاً. وتمامه في «درر الحكام»(2 : 27).
He is made a mukatab by purchase of his child and his parents, not one with whom there is no lineage connection. This according to Abu Hanifa. And according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]: if he purchases a mahram relative like a brother or uncle, he enters into his kitabah, just as he becomes free by his purchase. Abu Hanifa's proof: The mukatab has earning (kasb) and not ownership (milk); thus earning is made sufficient for connection in lineage proximity, because one capable of earning is addressed for maintenance in lineage, not in other [relations], since there is no necessity of wealth. Then: His saying: 'he is made a mukatab by purchase'; i.e., he becomes a mukatab like him. His saying: 'his child and his parents'; because he is qualified to be a mukatab, even if not qualified to free, so he is made a mukatab to fulfill blood tie as much as possible. From 'an-Nihaya'. His saying: 'not one with whom there is no lineage'; i.e., one with no lineage between them does not enter his kitabah. His saying: 'just as he becomes free by his purchase'; i.e., if the mukatab were free and purchased a brother or uncle, each one would be freed by his purchase. His saying: 'not ownership'; therefore charity is permissible for him, even if he acquires wealth, he does not own gift, and the marriage of his wife is not nullified if he purchases her. His saying: 'sufficient for connection'; he meant that his ability to earn necessitates connection in lineage proximity. From some marginal notes of al-Hidayah. And the strongest in entry is the child born in his kitabah, then the purchased child, then the parents; therefore they differ in rulings: the child born in his kitabah has the same ruling as his father, so if his father dies without leaving [payment], he works for the installments of his father; the purchased child pays the kitabah price immediately [or sold], otherwise he returns to slavery; the parents are returned to slavery and do not pay [any amount] immediately or deferred. Its completion in 'Durar al-Hukkam' (2:27).
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage addresses the question: When a mukatab purchases a relative, does that relative enter into his kitabah (contract of emancipation) or not? The matn states the ruling: only his child and parents are included in his kitabah by purchase; one with no direct lineage (e.g., brothers, uncles) does not enter. The commentator then explains the ikhtilaf. According to Abu Hanifa, the ruling is exactly as the matn says: only child and parents (i.e., those with direct lineal descent) enter. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), any mahram relative (like a brother or uncle) also enters the kitabah when purchased, just as they would be freed if the mukatab were free. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the mukatab only possesses earning (kasb) and not full ownership (milk). Hence, his ability to earn makes him capable of supporting only those related by birth (child, parents) because Shari'ah obligates maintenance for such relatives only when one has earnings; for other relatives, maintenance is not obligatory unless one has wealth (yasar). Since the mukatab does not own wealth, his purchase of non-lineal relatives does not include them in the kitabah. The footnotes elaborate: The child and parents enter because they are fit for kitabah even if not fit for freeing, to maintain blood ties. The note about 'strongest entry' clarifies the hierarchy: a child born in his kitabah is strongest, then a purchased child, then the parents, and each has different consequences regarding payment and status. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa restricts entry to direct lineage; the Sahibayn extend it to all mahram relatives. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly given here, but later Hanafi works typically adopt the view of the Sahibayn (that all mahram relatives enter) as the more practical and common ruling. However, the text here presents Abu Hanifa's view as the matn's position.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract (kitabah) with his master to earn and pay a specified sum for his freedom.
كتابة (kitabah): The contract between a slave and his master whereby the slave is obligated to pay a sum and upon payment becomes free.
ولاد (wilad): Lineage or birth relationship; direct parent-child connection.
كسب (kasb): Earning; the income the mukatab acquires through his labor.
ملك (milk): Ownership; complete proprietary right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His child and parents only
B) All blood relatives
C) Only his child
D) No relatives

Key Takeaway
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on entry into kitabah is resolved by preferring the Sahibayn's inclusive view, but the underlying principle of kasb vs milk remains central.

Sale of Mukatab's Umm Walad: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mukatab's sale of his umm walad, distinguish Abu Hanifa's view from the Sahibayn's, and apply the underlying principle of suspended earnings (kasb mawquf).

Core Idea
A mukatab can sell his umm walad only if he purchased her without the child—the child's inclusion blocks the sale.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a mukatab selling his umm walad. The matn of al-Wiqayah presents Abu Hanifa's position, while the Sahibayn disagree. The passage reveals how the principle of suspended earnings (kasb mawquf) interacts with the permanent status of motherhood, and how the mother's ruling follows the child's status (taba'iyyat al-walad). Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens one's grasp of the kitabah contract and the legal theory behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
Clearly define who is selling what: a mukatab (a slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom) is selling his umm walad (a female slave who has borne him a child). The key variable is whether the child was purchased together with the mother.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
From the matn: 'صح بيع أم ولده شراها بدونه، فإن شرى معه فلا.' – The sale is valid if he purchased her without the child; invalid if with the child. Identify the condition that determines validity.
3
Contrast the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the sale is never valid, based on the inherent prohibition against selling an umm walad. Their proof: 'لأنها أم ولده فلا يجوز بيعها' – because she is his umm walad, her sale is not permissible under any circumstance.
The opposing_school here is within the Hanafi madhhab itself.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the mukatab's earnings are suspended (kasb mawquf) – not fully owned until he completes payment. Therefore, these earnings cannot be attached to a permanent status (umm walad) that does not admit of rescission. By analogy, since the child is included in the kitabah (and will become free upon payment), the mother's status follows the child. If the child is not included, the mother can be sold.
Key principle: 'كسب المكاتب موقوف فلا يتعلق به ما لا يحتمل الفسخ'.
5
Apply the principle to the two scenarios
Scenario A: Mukatab purchased the mother without the child → sale valid (Abu Hanifa). Scenario B: Purchased mother with child → sale invalid by agreement (all three imams) because the child is in the kitabah and the mother is a dependent. Explain why the mother's sale is invalid in Scenario B even for Abu Hanifa.
The mother's saleability follows the child's status (taba'iyyat al-walad).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح بيع أم ولده شراها بدونه، فإن شرى معه فلا. هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما لا يصح بيعها، وإن شراها بدون الولد؛ لأنها أم ولده فلا يجوز بيعها. وله: أن القياس يجوز بيعها، وإن كان معها ولد؛ لأن كسب المكاتب موقوف فلا يتعلق به ما لا يحتمل الفسخ، أما إذا كان معها ولد يثبت امتناع البيع بتبعية الولد. قوله: وصح بيع أم ولده... الخ؛ يعني إذا ولدت زوجة المكاتب قبل أن يملكها بوجه من الوجوه، فملكها فإن ملكها مع الولد، فليس له أن يبيعها بالاتفاق؛ لأن ولدها داخل في الكتابة كما مر، والأم تابعة للولد في هذا الحكم على مقتضى الحديث، وإن ملكها بدونه له ذلك. كذا في الجلبي). قوله: بتبعية الولد... الخ؛ فإن قلت: إذا ثبت للولد حقيقة الحرية يثبت للأم حقها، وهاهنا يثبت للولد من حق الحرية، فينبغي أن لا يثبت للأم أنه تحقيقاً لانحطاط رتبتها عن الولد. قلت: للكتابة أحكام؛ منها عدم جواز البيع فيثبت للأم هذا الحكم دون الكتابة تحقيقاً لانحطاط رتبتها. فإن قلت: لم لا تصير مكاتبة تبعاً للولد. قلت: العقد ورد على المكاتب والولد جرؤه، فيكون وارداً عليه بخلاف الأم. كذا في بعض حواشي «الهداية». (۱) فلا تدخل في كتابته حتى لا تعتق بعتقه ولم ينفسخ النكاح؛ لأنه لم يملكها فجاز له أن يطأها بملك النكاح، وإن لم يكن الولد مع أم الولد جاز بيعها؛ لعدم دخولها في كتابته؛ لأن ما كسبه المكاتب متردد بين أن يؤدي وبين أن يعجز، فإن أدى الكل يتقرّر له، وإن عجز يتقرر للمولى فلا يتعلق به ما لا يحتمل الفسخ، وهو أمومية الولد. ينظر: «مجمع الأنهر»(٢ : ٤١١). (۲) في «ذخيرة العقبى» (ص٥٢٧). (۳) ينظر : «نتائج الأفكار» (۹ : ۱۸۱).
And the sale of his umm walad is valid if he purchased her without him [the child]; if he purchased her with him, then no. This according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two [Sahibayn], its sale is not valid, even if he purchased her without the child, because she is his umm walad and her sale is not permissible. Abu Hanifa's proof: Analogy permits her sale, even if she has a child with her, because the mukatab's earnings are suspended (mawquf) and therefore cannot be attached to that which does not admit of rescission. However, if she has a child with her, the prohibition of sale is established by following the child. His saying: 'And the sale of his umm walad is valid ...' means if the wife of the mukatab gave birth before he owned her in any way, and then he owned her; if he owned her with the child, he may not sell her by agreement, because her child is included in the kitabah as mentioned, and the mother follows the child in this ruling according to the hadith. And if he owned her without him, he has that [right]. From al-Jalabi. His saying: 'by following the child ...'; If it is said: When the child is established in true freedom, the mother's right is established; but here the child is established in some right of freedom, so the mother should not be established in [that right] because of the degradation of her rank relative to the child. I say: The rulings of kitabah include the impermissibility of sale; thus the mother is established in this ruling without [full] kitabah, to effect the degradation of her rank. If it is said: Why does she not become a mukatabah as a dependent of the child? I say: The contract was entered upon the mukatab, and the child is his offspring; so the contract applies to him, unlike the mother. From some marginal notes of al-Hidayah. (1) So she does not enter his kitabah, such that she is not freed by his emancipation and the marriage is not annulled; because he did not own her, so it is permissible for him to have intercourse with her by right of marriage. And if the child was not with the umm walad, her sale is permissible because she does not enter his kitabah; because what the mukatab earns is fluctuating between him paying and his being unable; if he pays all, it becomes his; if he is unable, it becomes the master's, so that which does not admit of rescission cannot be attached to it, and that is the mother-status (umoomiyyah) of the child. Refer to 'Majma' al-Anhur' (2:411). (2) In 'Dhakheera al-Uqba' (p.527). (3) Refer to 'Nata'ij al-Afkar' (9:181).
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of a mukatab selling his umm walad (a slave-concubine who has borne him a child). The matn states: 'The sale of his umm walad is valid if he purchased her without the child; but if he purchased her with the child, it is not valid.' This is the view of Abu Hanifa. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the sale is never valid, because the umm walad status itself prohibits her sale, regardless of the child's presence. Abu Hanifa's reasoning: The mukatab's earnings are suspended (mawquf) – they are not fully his until he completes payment, and if he fails, they revert to the master. Therefore, his earnings cannot be subject to something that does not admit of rescission (i.e., the mother-ship status of the child, which is permanent). By analogy (qiyas), since the child is already in the kitabah (and will become free upon payment), the mother follows the child's status; thus if the child is not included (i.e., purchased separately), the mother can be sold. The commentator elaborates: The mother's ruling is subordinate to the child's. If the child is purchased with the mother, the sale is prohibited by agreement (even according to Abu Hanifa) because the child is in the kitabah and the mother is dependent. The dialogue explains why the mother does not become a full mukatabah herself: because the kitabah contract is between the master and the mukatab; the child is part of the mukatab's family, but the mother is not automatically included. The footnotes clarify that if the child is not with the mother, she can be sold because she is not in the kitabah, and the uncertain nature of the mukatab's earnings prevents attaching the permanent umm walad status. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa allows sale when purchased without child; the Sahibayn disallow all sales of umm walad. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is unclear from this text, but later Hanafi works often adopt Abu Hanifa's view as more nuanced.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, thereby acquiring the status of a mother of the master's child; she becomes free upon the master's death.
كسب موقوف (kasb mawquf): Earnings that are suspended – not fully owned by the mukatab until he completes the kitabah; if he fails, they go to the master.
تبعية الولد (taba'iyyat al-walad): Following the child in legal rulings; the mother's status is subordinate to her child's status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for the validity of a mukatab's sale of his umm walad under Abu Hanifa's view?
A) The sale is valid regardless of whether the child is included.
B) The sale is valid only if the child is also sold together with the mother.
C) The sale is valid only if the mukatab purchased the mother without the child.
D) The sale is never valid.

Key Takeaway
A mukatab can sell his umm walad only if he purchased her without the child, because the child's inclusion in the kitabah makes the sale invalid by agreement.

Child of Mukatab from His Amah Enters His Kitabah
Ruling with proof for child entering kitabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mukatab's claimed child from his amah, identify the hadith proof, and explain how the ruling overrides analogy despite the prohibition on intercourse.

Core Idea
A mukatab's claimed child enters his kitabah by hadith, not analogy.

This lesson explores a nuanced ruling from the Book of Mukatab: if a mukatab (a slave under a kitabah contract) has a child born to his own female slave and claims the child, that child enters the same kitabah. This is surprising because the mukatab is not permitted to have intercourse with his slave, yet the child legally affects his contract. The Hanafi school accepts this as an exception based on a specific hadith, overriding the normal rules of analogy. We will unpack the proof, the objection, and the reasoning that sustains this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Note the act: a mukatab has a child from his amah and claims paternity. The ruling is that the child enters the mukatab's kitabah. This means the child's presence and earnings are tied to the father's manumission contract.
The child does not become free immediately; the kitabah contract includes the child's debt.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author cites the hadith: 'Her child emancipates her' (أعتقها ولدها). This is an ahad hadith with zanni thubut and dalala, but it is accepted as a binding exception.
The hadith is from Ibn Abbas and is considered sound enough to establish a ruling.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof applies)
The phrase 'her child emancipates her' indicates that a child born to a slave woman can affect the status of the mother (freedom). By extension, the child can also affect the father's kitabah—entering it as part of his property and earnings.
The analogy is not direct; the ruling is treated as an istihsan exception to qiyas.
4
Address the objection and rebuttal
The objection: the mukatab cannot have intercourse with his amah (according to the three scholars), so how can he have a child? The Hanafi response: the mukatab has authority (yad) over his slave like a free person; lineage is established upon claim even if the act was sinful. Analogies: jointly-owned slave girl and son's slave girl.
The key is yad (possession/authority), not permissibility of intercourse.
5
Determine the practical outcome
The child enters the kitabah, meaning the child's future earnings contribute to the kitabah payments. The child will be manumitted when the father completes his payments.
If the mukatab does not claim the child, the child remains the property of the master (implied).

Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hadith and commentary on a line from Al-Hidayah. The base ruling is that if a mukatab (a slave who has made a contract of kitabah with his master) has a child born from his own slave woman and he claims the child, that child enters into the same kitabah contract as the father. The author notes that this ruling is not established by primary sources (Quran or mutawatir Sunnah) and is against analogy (qiyas). However, it is based on a specific Hadith: the Prophet ﷺ said, 'Her child emancipates her.' The Hanafi school accepts this as an exception to the general rules of ownership and intercourse. A key objection is raised: a mukatab is not permitted to have intercourse with his slave woman (according to Abu Hanifa's companions and the other schools), so how can he have a child from her? The answer is that the mukatab has a type of authority (yad) over his slave like a free person, which is sufficient to establish lineage when he claims the child, even if the act of intercourse itself is not lawful. This is analogous to the cases of a jointly owned slave girl or a son's slave girl: if the father has intercourse and claims the child, the lineage is established despite the prohibition. Therefore, the child is considered the mukatab's property (as his earnings) and thus enters his kitabah.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract between a master and his slave whereby the slave agrees to pay a sum in installments in exchange for freedom upon full payment.
أمة (amah): A female slave owned by the mukatab.
يد (yad): Possession or authority; here used to mean the mukatab's legal power over his slave, sufficient to establish lineage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A Hadith: 'Her child emancipates her'
C) Analogy with the child of a free man
D) The mukatab's ownership of his slave woman for intercourse

Key Takeaway
A mukatab's claimed child from his amah enters his kitabah due to the hadith 'Her child emancipates her', overriding analogy and the prohibition on intercourse.

Child of Mukatab's Married Slaves and Earnings of the Child
Ruling and commentary on the child of a mukatab and the child of a mukatab's married slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings that (1) the child of a mukatab is his earning and its earnings belong to him, and (2) if a mukatab has a married slave couple and both become mukatabs, the child enters the mother's kitabah and the child's earnings belong to the mother, and explain the underlying principle of awsaf qarrah (permanent attributes running from mother to child).

Core Idea
A child follows the mother in permanent legal attributes like kitabah; the mukatab's child is his earning.

In Hanafi fiqh, the mukatab's unique position as a slave on a path to freedom creates specific rules regarding his offspring. This lesson examines two interconnected rulings: the general rule that a mukatab's child is considered his earning, and the specific case where a mukatab owns a married slave couple and both become mukatabs. The commentaries by al-Ayni, al-Tabyin, and al-Inayah clarify that the mother's legal attributes (kitabah, tadbir, istilad) run to her child, making the child follow her status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Locate the two distinct rulings in the passage: first, the earnings of the mukatab's child (Kasbuhu lahu); second, the case of a married slave couple (qinin) where the child enters the mother's kitabah and earnings belong to her.
The first ruling is general; the second is a specific application when both parents are mukatabs.
2
Understand the reasoning for the first ruling
The child is the kasb (earning) of the mukatab, so the child's earning is the earning of his earning – it belongs to the mukatab. This is based on the principle that a slave's acquisitions belong to his master, but here the mukatab is the master of his own earning.
The footnote adds that this applies before any claim of lineage.
3
Understand the reasoning for the second ruling
The child follows the mother because of awsaf qarrah (permanent legal attributes) that run from mother to child, such as kitabah, tadbir, and istilad. The mother's precedence is stronger because the child is 'part of her' (juz'uha).
The master's right over the child is cut off once the mother's kitabah applies.
4
Apply the same principle to other cases
The text extends this to a female mukatab giving birth from zina or from her husband: her child also enters her kitabah, because the attributes run from her.
Consistent application of awsaf qarrah.
5
Note the practical consequence
In the qinin case, the child's earnings go to the mother, not the father, because the mother's kitabah status overrides the father's general right to the child's earnings.
This shows the hierarchy: mother's attributes > father's earning right.
