وقبولُ الغائب له لغو، فإن كوتبت أمةٌ وطفلان لها فقبلت فأي أدى

[1] قوله: وقبول الغائب له لغو؛ يعني لا يؤثر قبوله في لزوم بدل الكتابة عليه، وكذلك ردّه لا يؤثر في ردّ عقد الكتابة عن الحاضر. الحاصل أنّ الكتابة لازمة للشاهد؛ أي العبد الحاضر؛ لأن الكتابة نفذت عليه من غير قبول الغائب، فلا يتغير بقبوله، كمن كفل من غيره بغير أمره فبلغه فأجازه، لا يتغير حكمه حتى لو أدى لا يرجع عليه، كذا هذا. هكذا في «الهداية» وحواشيها).

[2] قوله: فإن كوتبت أمة...الخ؛ أي إذا قبلت الأمة الكتابة عن نفسها وعن ابنين صغيرين لها جاز، إنّما وضع المسألة في الأمة إشارة إلى أن الحكم في العبد والأمة سواء فإنه لو وضعها في العبد لربما توهّم أنّ الجواز لثبوتِ ولاية الأب عليهما، فلا يجوز ذلك في الأمة؛ لعدم ولايتها، فإنّ الأم الحرّة لا ولاية لها، فكيف الأمة. كذا في «العناية».

[3] قوله: وطفلان لها؛ أي والحال أنّ لها ابنين صغيرين، وكذا إذا كان الولد واحداً، وإنما قيد بالصغير ليدل بذلك على أن لا أثر لقبول الغائب أو ردّه. كذا في «الكفاية».

[4] قوله: فأي أدى؛ أي أيهم أدى لم يرجع على صاحبه، ويجبر المولى على القبول؛ وذلك لأنَّ الأم إذا أدت فقد أدّت ديناً على نفسها، وكل من الولدين إن أدّى فهو متبرع غير مضطر، وفي ذلك كله لا رجوع. كذا في حاشية شرح الجلبي
And the acceptance of the absent one is void. If a female slave and her two young children are given kitaba, and she accepts, then whichever one pays... [1] He said: 'The acceptance of the absent one is void' – meaning his acceptance does not affect the obligation of the kitaba payment upon him, and likewise his rejection does not affect the cancellation of the kitaba contract from the present one. The result is that the kitaba is binding on the witness (i.e., the present slave) because the contract was executed upon him without the acceptance of the absent one, so it does not change by his acceptance. This is like one who guarantees for another without his order, then the news reaches him and he approves; the ruling does not change, even if he pays, he does not recover from him. So this. As in al-Hidayah and its glosses. [2] He said: 'If a female slave is given kitaba...' – that is, if she accepts the kitaba for herself and her two young sons, it is permissible. He placed the issue with the female slave to indicate that the ruling for a male slave and a female slave is the same, because if he placed it with the male, one might imagine that the permissibility is due to the father's guardianship over them, so it would not be permissible for the female slave due to her lack of guardianship. Indeed, a free mother has no guardianship, so how about a female slave? As in al-Inaya. [3] He said: 'And two young children for her' – meaning that she has two young sons. Likewise, if the child is one. He specified 'young' to indicate that the acceptance or rejection of the absent one has no effect. As in al-Kifaya. [4] He said: 'Then whichever one pays' – meaning whichever of them pays does not recover from his companion, and the master is compelled to accept. This is because if the mother pays, she pays a debt upon herself; and if either of the sons pays, he is a volunteer not compelled. In all this, there is no recourse. As in the gloss of Sharh al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Book of Mukatab concerning the kitaba contract involving a female slave and her young children. The matn states: 'The acceptance of the absent one is void.' The commentator explains that when a female slave (umm walad or ordinary slave) is given kitaba together with her two young sons, and she accepts the contract for herself and on their behalf, the acceptance of the absent children is ineffective; the contract only binds the present person (the mother). The sons are considered 'gha'ib' (absent) because they are not legally competent to accept or reject due to their young age. The acceptance by the mother does not impose any obligation on them; they are not bound to pay the kitaba. If any of them pays, they do not recover from each other. The mother pays her own debt; the sons, if they pay, are volunteers (mutabarri'), not compelled, so they cannot recover from the mother or from each other. The master must accept the payment from whomever offers it. The commentator then shows that the ruling is the same for a male slave or free father; the distinction is to remove the misconception that a father has guardianship. The example of a female slave emphasizes that even a female slave lacks guardianship, so her acceptance for her children is not binding on them.

Key Terms
المكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitaba) with his master, agreeing to pay a specified sum in exchange for freedom.
القبول (al-qabul): The acceptance of the kitaba offer by the slave, which is a pillar (rukn) of the contract.
اللغو (al-laghw): Legally ineffective; having no legal effect. Here, the acceptance of the absent child is laghw.
الأمة (al-ummah): A female slave. In this context, the mother of the children being given kitaba.
الطفلان (al-tiflan): Two young children (twins) of the female slave, still minors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the acceptance of the young children (tiflan) in the kitaba contract considered 'laghw' (void)?
A) Because the master did not accept their acceptance.
B) Because they are not present at the contract and lack legal capacity.
C) Because the mother cannot act as their guardian.
D) Because the children are free, not slaves.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the acceptance of an absent minor in a kitaba contract is laghw (void); the contract binds only the present slave, and any payment by the child is voluntary without right of recourse.

Acceptance of the Absent in Kitabah is Void
Commentary on voidness of absent acceptance in kitabah with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on acceptance of an absent party in kitabah and explain the consequences using the example of a female slave and her children.

Core Idea
The absent's acceptance or rejection is void — the kitabah binds only the present party.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi principle in the contract of kitabah: the acceptance of an absent party has no legal effect. This ruling, supported by analogy to unauthorized guarantee (kafalah), ensures that contracts are concluded only by those present. We will explore a concrete case of a female slave writing herself and her young children, clarifying the legal relationships of principality and dependency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Recognize that the text declares acceptance of an absent party as 'laghw' (void). The contract was executed solely on the present party.
The rejection of the absent also has no effect.
2
Trace the Analogy to Kafalah
Compare with a guarantee without authorization: subsequent approval does not change the binding on the guarantor, just as the absent's acceptance does not affect the kitabah.
This is a qiyas khafi analogy.
3
Analyze the Female Slave Example
In the example, the mother is principal (asl) and children are dependents (taba'). Her acceptance is sufficient; their input is irrelevant.
The ruling is the same for a male or female slave.
4
Determine the Legal Effect of Payment
Whichever pays — mother or child — all are freed. The payer cannot seek recourse because the child's payment is voluntary (tabarru'), and the mother's payment is her own debt.
Dependency (taba'iyyah) makes the child's payment equivalent to the mother's.

و مجامي
Acceptance of the absent is void. So if a female slave and her two children are written (i.e., enter kitabah) and she accepts, then whichever (of them) pays... (They will be freed) and will not have recourse. (Acceptance of the absent is void because the contract was executed on the present party.) [1] His saying: 'So if a female slave and her two children are written... she accepts, then whichever pays' with the presumption of obtaining freedom for him. This is like the saying 'lack of profit is not called loss'. So in 'Hashiyat al-Jalabi'. [1] His saying: 'Acceptance of the absent is void' means his acceptance does not affect the binding of the kitabah payment upon him, and likewise his rejection does not affect the nullification of the contract from the present. The conclusion is that the kitabah is binding on the witness, i.e., the present slave, because the kitabah was executed upon him without the acceptance of the absent, so it does not change by his acceptance, just as one who sponsors (kafala) for another without his permission, then it reaches him and he approves it, does not change its ruling, even if he pays he does not take recourse against him. This is similar. So in 'Al-Hidayah' and its glosses. [2] His saying: 'So if a female slave and her two children are written...' i.e., if the female slave accepts the kitabah for herself and for her two young sons, it is valid. He only set the case with a female slave to indicate that the ruling for a male slave and a female slave is the same, because had he set it with a male slave, one might mistakenly think that permissibility is due to the father's guardianship over them (the children), so it would not be permissible for the female slave due to her lack of guardianship, for the free mother has no guardianship, so how about the female slave? So in 'Al-'Inayah'. [3] His saying: 'and her two children' i.e., and the situation is that she has two young sons, and likewise if the child is one. He qualified it as young to indicate that the acceptance or rejection of the absent has no effect. So in 'Al-Kifayah'. [4] His saying: 'so whichever pays' i.e., whichever of them pays does not take recourse against his companion, and the master is compelled to accept; because if the mother pays, she has paid a debt on herself, and each of the two children if he pays, he is a volunteer (mutabarri') without compulsion, and in all that there is no recourse. So in Hashiyat Sharh al-Jalabi. 'They will not have recourse and they are freed' as in the first issue. [1] His saying: 'and they are freed' because she made herself principal (asl) in the kitabah, and her children dependents (taba'), as we explained in the first issue, and she is more entitled to that than a stranger, meaning that this contract in this manner is valid with respect to a stranger, so with respect to her child it is more fitting, because her child is closer to her than a stranger. So in 'Al-Hidayah' and its commentaries. (2) His saying: 'as in the first issue' which is the kitabah of the present slave for himself and the absent slave. If you say: When one of them pays, the other son should not be freed, because there is no principality or dependency between them. I say: If one of them pays, his payment is like the mother's payment, because he is dependent on her in every respect. If the mother pays, they are all freed, so likewise if one of them pays. So in Sharh al-'Ayni. And ... (incomplete).
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a supercommentary (hashiya/sharh) expanding on the ruling that the acceptance of an absent party in a kitabah contract is legally void (laghw). The author explains that the contract was executed only on the present slave; therefore, any subsequent acceptance or rejection by an absent party—such as another slave or a child—has no effect on the binding nature of the contract. This is analogous to the case of a guarantee (kafalah) where a person sponsors without authorization; even if the absent party later approves, the ruling does not change and the sponsor cannot claim reimbursement. The text then gives a concrete example: a female slave enters a kitabah contract for herself and her two young children. She accepts. Whichever of them pays the agreed sum—whether the mother herself or one of the children—they are all freed, and the payer has no right of recourse against the others. The author clarifies why the example uses a female slave: to show that the ruling is the same for a male or female slave, and to dispel the misconception that permissibility stems from the father's guardianship—since a female slave has no guardianship, yet it is still valid. The key point is that the mother is considered the principal (asl) and the children are dependents (taba'), so any payment (whether by mother or child) is treated as if the mother paid, leading to freedom for all without recourse. This ruling is based on the principle that the absent party's lack of involvement does not affect the contract; the present party's acceptance is sufficient. The passage concludes with an objection and answer: if a child pays, why should the other child be freed? The answer: the child's payment is like the mother's payment because he is her dependent; therefore, it operates as a payment from her, freeing everyone. This lesson reinforces the Hanafi position on the binding nature of kitabah for the present party and the legal irrelevance of absent parties.

Key Terms
كتابة (kitabah): A contract whereby a slave agrees to pay a sum to the master in installments in exchange for freedom upon full payment.
لغو (laghw): Legally void; an act that has no legal effect. Here, the acceptance or rejection of an absent party in kitabah is declared laghw, meaning it does not influence the contract's validity or bindingness.
أصالة (asalah): Principality; being the primary party in a contract. In this passage, the mother is the principal (أصالة) in the kitabah, while the children are dependents (تبعية).
تبعية (taba'iyyah): Dependency; being a subordinate in a legal relationship. The children are dependents of the mother in the kitabah contract, so their payment is treated as her payment.
متبرع (mutabarri'): One who acts voluntarily without compulsion. In the passage, if a child pays the kitabah, it is considered a voluntary payment (تبرع) and he cannot claim reimbursement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only that child is freed, and the mother and other child remain slaves.
B) All three are freed, but the paying child can claim reimbursement from the mother.
C) All three are freed, and no one has a right of recourse against the others.
D) The mother and paying child are freed, but the other child remains a slave.

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of an absent party in kitabah is legally void; the contract binds only the present party, and payments by dependents are voluntary without recourse.

Kitaba of a Jointly Owned Slave: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Divisibility
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kitaba divisibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on kitaba divisibility and apply the ruling on distribution of collected payment when authorization is given.

Core Idea
Kitaba is divisible per Abu Hanifa; indivisible per Sahibayn — collected money goes entirely to the authorized partner per Abu Hanifa.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the nature of the kitaba contract when a slave is jointly owned. The core issue is whether kitaba is divisible (mujazzah) or indivisible, which affects who receives the payment collected after the slave becomes unable to complete the kitaba. Understanding this dispute highlights how a foundational principle (divisibility) leads to different practical outcomes.

Guided Path
1
State the Scenario
Identify the key elements: a jointly owned slave, one partner authorizes the other to write a kitaba for his share and collect payment. The authorized partner executes the kitaba and collects part of the sum, then the slave defaults.
2
Identify the Hanafi Position (Abu Hanifa)
Abu Hanifa rules that the collected amount belongs entirely to the authorized partner. The basis is that kitaba is divisible (mujazzah), so the authorization is confined to the authorizer's share.
The pronoun in 'his share' refers to the other partner.
3
Identify the Opposing Position (Sahibayn)
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the collected amount is shared between the two partners. Their basis is that kitaba is not divisible, so the authorization extends to the whole slave.
They view the authorized partner as principal for his own share and agent for the other.
4
Analyze the Underlying Principle
The dispute hinges on whether the kitaba contract can be split by ownership shares. Abu Hanifa's view treats each share independently; Sahibayn view the contract as a single indivisible unit.
This is a purely juridical difference (ikhtilaf fi al-hukm) within the same school.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah)
In practice, the distribution of collected payment differs: under Abu Hanifa the collector keeps all; under Sahibayn it is shared. A mufti must decide which view to adopt.
The text does not give a tarjih; both views are transmitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب كتابة العبد المشترك
أحد شريكي عبد أذن للآخر بكتابة حصته بألف وقبضه ففعل وقبض بعضه فذا له إن عجز
قال: الضمير في حصته يرجع إلى الآخر، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وأصله: أن الكتابة متجزئة فيكون مقتصراً على نصيبه. وعندهما: الكتابة غير متجزئة، فالإذن بكتابة نصيبه إذن بكتابة الكل، فالقابض أصيل في البعض، ووكيل في البعض، والمقبوض مشترك بينهما، فبقي كذلك بعد العجز.
Chapter of Kitaba of the Jointly Owned Slave: One of the two partners of a slave authorized the other to write a kitaba of his share for one thousand dirhams and to collect it. He did so and collected some of it. Then if the slave becomes unable to pay, that collected amount belongs to the collector. He said: The pronoun in 'his share' refers to the other partner. This is according to Abu Hanifa. His principle is that kitaba is divisible, so it is confined to his share. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), kitaba is not divisible, so authorization to write a kitaba of his share is authorization to write a kitaba of the whole. Thus the collector is a principal in part and an agent in part, and the collected amount is shared between them, remaining so after the slave's inability to pay.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the nature of the kitaba contract. The matn states the ruling: if one co-owner authorizes the other to write a kitaba for his share and collect the payment, and the authorized partner executes the kitaba and collects part of the sum, then if the slave becomes unable to pay, the collected amount belongs entirely to the collector. The author explains that this is the position of Abu Hanifa, based on the principle that kitaba is divisible (muta'azzin). Therefore, the authorization only affects the authorizer's share. In contrast, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that kitaba is not divisible, so the authorization for one share extends to the entire slave. Consequently, the collector acts as a principal for his own share and as an agent for the other partner, and the collected money is shared between them, even after the slave's inability to pay.

Key Terms
كتابة (kitābah): A contract by which a slave agrees to pay a sum to the master in installments in exchange for freedom.
متجزئة (mutajazzi’ah): Divisible; referring to the ability to separate ownership shares in the kitaba contract.
أذن (idhn): Authorization or permission given by one partner to the other to act regarding the kitaba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is shared equally between the partners.
B) It goes entirely to the authorized partner.
C) It goes to the slave.
D) It is split according to ownership shares.

Key Takeaway
The distribution of kitaba payments upon default depends on the divisibility of the contract: Abu Hanifa gives all to the collector; Sahibayn share it between the partners.

Ruling on a Jointly Owned Mukataba with Children Claimed by Partners
Presents a case of a jointly owned mukataba bearing children claimed by each partner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the status of children born to a jointly owned mukataba when each partner claims paternity, and explain the effect on the kitaba when she fails to pay.

Core Idea
When each partner claims a different child, both children become free and the kitaba is cancelled upon her inability.

This lesson examines a specific case from the kitaba chapter: two men jointly own a mukataba who gives birth to two children, each claimed by a different partner. Then she becomes unable to pay. Understanding this helps you grasp how istilad (paternity claim) interacts with joint ownership and the kitaba contract in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their status
Two men jointly own a mukataba (each owns half). She is under a kitaba contract to purchase her freedom. She bears two children – first a child claimed by partner A, then another child claimed by partner B.
The mukataba remains a slave until the kitaba is fully paid.
2
Apply the rule of istilad
In Hanafi fiqh, if a master claims paternity of a child born to his female slave, the child becomes free and lineage is established. Each partner claims the child born during his period of ownership.
The claim must be explicit and during the mother's slave status.
3
Determine the effect on each child
Both children are free. The first child is attributed to partner A, the second to partner B. Each child becomes a freeborn son/daughter of the claiming partner.
The children do not inherit the mother's slave status because of the paternity claim.
4
Determine the effect on the kitaba
The mother then becomes unable to pay (ajazat). The kitaba is invalidated and she returns to full slavery, jointly owned by both partners as before. The free children remain free; their fathers own no part of them.
Compensation: Each claiming partner must pay the co-owner half the value of the claimed child (since the co-owner lost his share in that child). These compensations may offset each other if the children are of equal value.

Source Text

مكاتبة لرجلين جاءت بولد فادعاه أحدهما ثم جاءت بآخر فادعاه الآخر فعجزت
A female slave under kitaba jointly owned by two men gave birth to a child, and one of them claimed paternity. Then she gave birth to another, and the other claimed paternity, and then she became unable to pay.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This matn presents a new case for the chapter on mukatab. It describes a jointly owned mukataba who bears two children in succession, each child claimed by a different partner. The matn ends by stating that she became unable to pay (ajazat). The full legal consequences are not detailed in this passage, but the expected ruling would address the status of the children (who becomes the master of each child based on the claim) and how the kitaba is affected. In the Hanafi school, if a mukataba bears a child, the child follows the mother in being a mukatab if she is still under kitaba, unless the master claims paternity, which changes the child's status. The scenario here adds complexity due to joint ownership and separate paternity claims.

Key Terms
مكاتبة (mukātabah): A female slave who has entered into a kitaba contract to purchase her freedom.
ادعاء الولد (id‘ā’ al-walad): Claiming paternity of a child born to a female slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The child becomes the property of the claiming partner.
B) The child is free and the claim establishes lineage.
C) The child remains a slave until the kitaba is paid.
D) The child becomes owned by both partners equally.

Key Takeaway
When each partner claims a different child of a jointly owned mukataba, both children are free, and the mother's kitaba is cancelled upon her inability to pay.

Shared Mukatabah: Abū Ḥanīfa vs. Ṣāḥibayn on Istīlād
Intra-Ḥanafī dispute with legal consequences for both co-owners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the divisibility of istīlād of a jointly owned mukātabah and explain the practical difference between Abū Ḥanīfa and the Ṣāḥibayn.

Core Idea
Istīlād of a mukātabah is divisible for Abū Ḥanīfa, indivisible for the Ṣāḥibayn—the child's status and compensation depend on this.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of istīlād (sexual intercourse claiming paternity) with a jointly owned mukātabah (a slave under a kitābah contract). The passage explains Abū Ḥanīfa's view that the act is divisible and its consequences, and contrasts it with the Ṣāḥibayn's (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) position that it is indivisible. Understanding this dispute clarifies how the later inability of the mukātabah to pay (ʿajz) retroactively changes the legal status of the woman and children.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
What is istīlād? Who are the co-owners? What is a mukātabah? Establish the legal context.
2
State Abū Ḥanīfa's ruling for the first co-owner
What happens when the first co-owner performs istīlād initially? Why is it divisible? Note that it is confined to his share.
Because the mukātab does not transfer from ownership to ownership.
3
Trace the second co-owner's scenario
What if the second co-owner also performs istīlād before ʿajz? What changes when ʿajz occurs? Understand the retroactive indivisibility.
After ʿajz, the first's istīlād becomes indivisible, making her entirely his umm walad.
4
State the Ṣāḥibayn's view
How is it different? What is the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)?
The second's share transfers to the first, and the first owns her entirely.
5
Evaluate the legal reasoning
Why does the divisibility matter? How does the unique status of the mukātabah justify Abū Ḥanīfa's position?
The mukātab is neither full slave nor free, affecting the nature of istīlād.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهي أم ولد للأول وضمن نصف قيمتها، ونصف عقرها، وشريكه عقرها، وقيمة الولد وهو ابنه هذا عند أبي حنيفة. وبيانه: أن استيلاد المكاتبة المشتركة متجزى عند أبي حنيفة، فيقتصر على نصيبه؛ لأن المكاتب لا ينتقل من ملك إلى ملك كما مر في المدبر، واستيلاد القنة لا يتجزأ، فإذا استولد أحد الشريكين القنة المشتركة، صارت كلها أم ولد له، ويضمن نصف قيمتها للشريك. وإذا عرفت هذا، فاستيلاد الثاني قبل العجز وقع في ملكه ظاهراً فيثبت نسب ولده، لكن إذا عجزت صارت كان الكتابة لم تكن، فظهر أنه في الحقيقة وطئ أم ولد الغير، فاستيلاد الأول وقع غير متجزئ، فكلها أم ولد له، ويضمن نصف قيمتها لشريكه، ولا تكون أم ولد للشريك، لكن ولد الشريك ولد مغرور، حيث وطئ معتمداً على الملك، فيكون حراً بالقيمة، ويضمن تمام عقرها. وأما عندهما فاستيلاد المكاتبة لا يتجزأ، فقبل العجز صارت أم ولد للأول، وانتقل نصيب الثاني إليه بفسخ الكتابة.
Thus she is an umm walad for the first (co-owner), and he guarantees half her value, and half her 'aqr, and (he also guarantees) the 'aqr of his partner, and the value of the child, who is his son—this is according to Abū Ḥanīfa. Its explanation: The istīlād of a jointly owned mukātabah is divisible according to Abū Ḥanīfa, so it is confined to his share, because the mukātab does not transfer from ownership to ownership as previously mentioned regarding the mudabbar, while the istīlād of a female slave (qinn) is indivisible. So if one of the two co-owners performs istīlād on a jointly owned female slave, she becomes entirely his umm walad, and he guarantees half her value to the partner. Once you understand this: the istīlād of the second (co-owner) before her 'ajz (inability to pay) occurs apparently in his ownership, so the lineage of his child is established. But if she later becomes unable to pay, it is as if the kitābah never existed, so it becomes evident that in reality he had intercourse with the umm walad of another. Thus the istīlād of the first becomes indivisible, so she is entirely his umm walad, and he guarantees half her value to his partner, and she does not become umm walad for the partner. However, the child of the partner is a child of a deceived person (mugharrar) since he had intercourse relying on ownership, so he is free upon payment of value, and he guarantees the full 'aqr of her. As for according to them (the two Ṣāḥibayn), the istīlād of the mukātabah is indivisible, so before 'ajz she becomes umm walad for the first, and the share of the second transfers to him by dissolution of the kitābah.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Ḥanafī dispute on the effect of istīlād (sexual intercourse claiming paternity) with a jointly owned mukātabah (a slave who has contracted kitābah to earn freedom). The ruling in the matn states that the first claimant's child makes her his umm walad, he pays half value and half 'aqr, and also pays the full 'aqr and child's value for the second. Abū Ḥanīfa argues that istīlād of a mukātabah is divisible (mutajazzī) because the mukātab cannot be transferred from one ownership to another—she is not fully owned as a slave. Therefore, each co-owner's istīlād only affects his own share. If the second co-owner also claims a child before the mukātabah becomes unable to pay, his child's lineage is established due to apparent ownership. However, if she later becomes unable to pay, the kitābah is negated, revealing that his intercourse was with another's umm walad, so the first's istīlād becomes indivisible (like a regular slave), making her entirely his umm walad. The second's child is treated as that of a mugharrar (deceived one)—free upon payment of value—and he must pay the full 'aqr. In contrast, the two Ṣāḥibayn (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) hold that istīlād of a mukātabah is indivisible; thus, before 'ajz, she becomes umm walad for the first, and the second's share transfers to him by dissolution of the kitābah.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears a child recognized by her master, freeing her upon his death.
استيلاد (istīlād): The act of a master having intercourse with his female slave in a manner that establishes paternity of the child.
مكاتبة (mukātabah): A female slave under a kitābah contract to purchase her freedom in installments.
مغرور (mugharrar): A person who acts under a mistaken belief of ownership, here the second co-owner who thought he owned the slave.
عقر ('aqr): The dower or compensation paid for intercourse with a slave woman, analogous to mahr for a free woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abū Ḥanīfa, what happens to the child of the second co-owner if the mukātabah later becomes unable to pay?
A) The child becomes a slave of the first co-owner.
B) The child is free but the second co-owner must pay its value.
C) The child is free with no compensation.
D) The child becomes a slave of the second co-owner.

Key Takeaway
The divisibility of istīlād of a mukātabah determines whether the second co-owner's child is free with compensation or the co-ownership is dissolved; this intra-Hanafi dispute highlights the unique legal middle ground of the mukātab.

Void Tadbir by Co-Owner When Mukataba Becomes Unable
Ruling on tadbir by co-owner after istilad and 'ajz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a co-owner's tadbir is void when the mukataba becomes unable, and explain the difference in legal reasoning between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the timing of ownership.

Core Idea
A tadbir is void if the master lacks full ownership at the time of declaration, even if ownership arises retroactively.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario from the Book of al-Mukatabah: a co-owned mukataba who has been made an umm walad by one co-owner, then the other co-owner gives her tadbir without intercourse, and she later becomes unable to pay. The text rules that the tadbir is void and she becomes entirely umm walad for the first owner. We will analyze the reasoning behind this ruling, including the difference between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples on the timing of ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the act
The scenario involves two co-owners of a mukataba. The first owner has intercourse producing istilad (making her an umm walad for his share). The second owner pays the 'uqr (compensation) but does not have intercourse; instead, he performs tadbir (declares her free upon his death). Later, the mukataba becomes unable to pay her kitaba (al-ʿajz). The act in question is the tadbir of the second owner.
The 'uqr payment is valid because the mukataba owns her own benefits before incapacity.
2
State the ruling
The tadbir is batil (void) and the slave girl becomes entirely an umm walad for the first owner, meaning the first owner's right to her is established fully. The second owner loses his right to half the kitaba price.
The ruling is explicitly given in the text: 'بطل تدبيره، وهي أم ولد للأول'.
3
Trace the proof (dalil)
The proof is the general principle that tadbir requires full ownership of the slave at the time of declaration. At the moment of tadbir, the second owner did not have full ownership because the first owner had rights from istilad that later became complete.
The text states: 'لأنه لم يصادف الملك' (it did not coincide with ownership).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the cause
Both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree the tadbir is void, but they differ on why. The Sahibayn say the first owner owned her from the moment of istilad (before 'ajz), so at the time of tadbir she was not owned by the second. Abu Hanifa says ownership of the first is established retroactively upon 'ajz, so it becomes clear that the tadbir coincided with another's ownership.
This difference does not change the outcome, but affects other cases (e.g., if the second sold his share before 'ajz).
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical result is the same: the tadbir is void, and she is umm walad for the first. However, the difference in reasoning matters for other transactions. For example, if the second owner had sold his share before 'ajz, the Sahibayn would hold the sale invalid (since she was already owned by the first), while Abu Hanifa would hold the sale valid subject to later retroactive adjustment.
The text emphasizes that the kitaba contract is not fully dissolved; it only dissolves regarding the right of ownership for the second, so the first owner still receives the full kitaba price.

Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Book of al-Mukatabah. It states the rule for a scenario where a mukataba slave woman is co-owned by two partners. If one partner (the first) has intercourse and causes istilad (making her an umm walad for his share), and then the second partner pays the 'uqr (compensation for the first's right to intercourse, as the mukataba is entitled to her own benefits) but does not have intercourse, and instead performs tadbir (declaring her free upon his death), and then she becomes unable to pay her kitaba (عجزت), the following rulings apply: the tadbir is void (batil), and she becomes an umm walad for the first owner completely (i.e., for his full share). The second owner is harmed by losing his right to half the kitaba price. The commentary explains the reasoning. First, the payment of 'uqr is valid before 'ajz because the mukataba is entitled to her own benefits and their compensations. Second, the tadbir is void because it occurred when the second owner did not have full ownership over her. There is a difference of opinion between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) on the precise reason: according to the Sahibayn, the first owner owned her before 'ajz due to istilad, so at the time of tadbir she was already owned by the first. According to Abu Hanifa, it is only upon 'ajz that it becomes known that the first owner owned his share from the time of intercourse, thus retroactively the tadbir coincided with someone else's ownership. Despite this difference, both agree the tadbir is void. The child born from the first owner belongs to him (as he owns the share), and upon 'ajz, the kitaba is deemed never to have existed, and she becomes entirely his umm walad. The passage also clarifies that the kitaba contract continues with full price for the first owner after 'ajz, because it only dissolved regarding the right of ownership (for the second), but not regarding the payment.

Key Terms
المكاتبة (al-mukātabah): A female slave who has entered into a kitaba contract with her master to earn her freedom by paying a fixed sum.
التدبير (al-tadbīr): A unilateral declaration by a master that a slave will be free upon the master's death.
الاستيلاد (al-istīlād): The act of a master causing his female slave to give birth to his child, which makes her an umm walad.
العقر (al-ʿuqar): Compensation paid for having intercourse with a female slave, typically paid to the owner or to the mukataba if she is entitled to her benefits.
البدل (al-badal): The price of the kitaba contract, i.e., the amount the mukataba agrees to pay for her freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The tadbir is valid and she becomes free upon his death.
B) The tadbir is void and she becomes wholly umm walad for the first owner.
C) The tadbir is void but she remains a mukataba for both.
D) The tadbir is suspended until she pays.

Key Takeaway
A tadbir by a co-owner is void if another co-owner has prior istilad rights that become complete upon the mukataba's 'ajz.

Guarantee and Recoupment Upon Freeing a Co-Owned Mukataba
Ruling with proof on guarantee and recoupment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on the guarantee of value and the right of recoupment when one co-owner frees a co-owned mukataba who later becomes incapable.

Core Idea
Freeing a co-owned mukataba obliges the guarantor to pay half her value and recover it from her upon her incapacity.

This passage examines a specific scenario within the rules of co-ownership and mukataba (contract of manumission by payment). When one co-owner frees his share of a mukataba while she is still under contract, and she later becomes unable to pay, the freeing owner must compensate his partner for the value of the partner's share. Importantly, he can then recover this amount from the mukataba herself. This rule stems from the Hanafi principle that emancipation is divisible and that ownership by istilad (intercourse) creates a retroactive obligation. We will walk through the legal reasoning and the positions of the early Hanafi masters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
First, establish the facts: two co-owners make a kitaba contract with a slave woman (mukataba). One of them then frees her (i.e., remits his share of the kitaba) while he is wealthy. Later, the mukataba becomes incapable of paying the remaining kitaba. What are the legal consequences?
The freeing is from his share only; the other co-owner does not free.
2
Determine the Guarantee Obligation
Upon the mukataba's 'ajz (incapacity), the freeing owner must guarantee (daman) half the value of the slave woman to his partner. The reason is that the incapacity retroactively reveals that the freeing owner actually owned the partner's share from the time of istilad (if there was intercourse), so he must compensate the partner for the value of that share.
The guarantee is for the value at the time of the event (istilad or 'ajz).
3
Trace the Reasoning (Ta'lil)
The text explains that the child belongs to the first owner (the one who caused istilad) because his paternity claim is valid: he owned a share at the time of istilad, and istilad of a mukataba is valid by consensus. However, his act of istilad retroactively transfers the partner's share to him by value, so the tadbir (second owner's declaration) becomes invalid. This chain of reasoning establishes the guarantee.
The lineage depends on deception (al-ghurur), so it is treated differently.
4
Note the Right of Recoupment (Ruju')
The freeing owner not only must pay the partner but also has the right to recover the amount from the mukataba after she becomes capable or upon her 'ajz. This is based on the idea that she remains responsible for her own kitaba, and the guarantee is like a payment on her behalf.
Abu Hanifa holds this view; the Sahibayn may differ on the timing.
5
Recognize the Intra-Hanafi Difference
The text notes that before 'ajz, the freeing owner cannot be forced to guarantee, because emancipation is divisible according to Abu Hanifa: it makes the non-emancipated share like that of a mukatab, leaving the partner's share unchanged. The 'ajz changes the situation retroactively.
This difference is between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والولد له ، وضَمِنَ لشريكه نصف عقرها ، ونصف قيمتها، فإن حررها أحدهما غنيّاً فعجزتْ ضَمِنَ نصف قيمتها لشريكه، ورجع به عليها
والولد له ، وضَمِنَ لشريكه نصف عقرها ، ونصف قيمتها) ؛ لأنه تبين بالعجز أنه يملك نصيب الشريك وقت الاستيلاد ، فالتدبير وقع في غير ملكه بخلافِ النسب ؛ لأنه يعتمد الغرور. (فإن حررها ) : أي المكاتبة المشتركة ، أحدهما غنيّاً فعجزت ضَمِنَ نصف قيمتها لشريكه، ورجع به عليها)
في قول أبي حنيفة له ، ثم إذا عجزت بعد ذلك جعلت الكتابة كأن لم تكن، وتبيَّن أنّ الجارية كلّها أم ولد للأوّل ؛ لأنه زالَ المانع من الانتقال. كذا في «الكفاية»). 11 قوله : والولد له ؛ أي للأول ؛ لأنه صحت دعوته لقيام المصحح وهو الملك في المكاتبة ؛ لأن استيلاده كان عند قيام الكتابة، ويصح استيلاد المكاتبة بالإجماع ؛ لأن الاختلاف مع بقاء الكتابة، وهاهنا ما بقيت لأنه لما استولدها الأول ملك نصف شريكه، ولم يبق ملك للمدبّر فيها، فلا يصح تدبيره. كذا في «الكفاية ) ، و«العناية» (۳).
[٢]قوله : ونصف قيمتها ؛ لأنه تملك نصفها بالاستيلاد، وهو تملك بالقيمة. [٣] قوله : فإن حررها ؛ صورته : إن كاتب الجارية المشتركة ثمّ أعتقها أحدهما وهو موسر، ثم عجزت يضمن المعتق لشريكه نصف قيمتها، ويرجع بذلك عليها. [٤]قوله : فعجزت ; وأما قبل العجز ليس له أن يضمن المعتق عند الأعظم له ؛ لأنَّ الإعتاق لما كان يتجزؤ عنده كان أثره أن يجعل نصيب غير المعتق كالمكاتب، فلا يتغيّر بالإعتاق نصيب صاحبه ؛ لأنها مكاتبة قبل ذلك.
And the child is for him, and he guarantees to his partner half her 'uqr and half her value. Then if one of them frees her while being wealthy and she becomes incapable, he guarantees half her value to his partner and recovers it from her. (And the child is for him, and he guarantees to his partner half her 'uqr and half her value) because it becomes clear through incapacity that he owned the partner's share at the time of istilad, so the tadbir occurred in non-ownership, unlike lineage because it depends on deception. (Then if he frees her): i.e., the co-owned mukataba, one of them while wealthy and she becomes incapable, he guarantees half her value to his partner and recovers it from her, according to the saying of Abu Hanifa for him. Then when she becomes incapable after that, the kitaba is made as if it never was, and it becomes clear that the slave girl is entirely an umm walad for the first, because the impediment to transfer has been removed. Thus in al-Kifayah. 11. His saying: And the child is for him; i.e., for the first, because his claim is valid due to the presence of the basis, which is ownership in the kitaba, because his istilad occurred while the kitaba was in effect, and istilad of a mukataba is valid by consensus, because the difference (of opinion) is about (the continuation of) the kitaba. And here it did not remain, because when the first caused istilad, he owned half of the partner, and there is no ownership remaining for the mudabbir over her, so his tadbir is not valid. Thus in al-Kifayah and al-'Inayah. [2] His saying: And half her value; because he owned half of her through istilad, and it is ownership by value. [3] His saying: Then if he frees her; its form: if he makes a kitaba contract for the co-owned slave girl, then one of them frees her while he is wealthy, then she becomes incapable, the emancipator guarantees half her value to his partner and recovers that from her. [4] His saying: She becomes incapable; as for before incapacity, he cannot guarantee the emancipator according to the greatest (scholar), because emancipation when it is divisible in his view has the effect of making the non-emancipated share like a mukatab, so the share of the partner does not change by emancipation, because she was a mukataba before that.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of co-owned mukataba. It begins by stating that the child belongs to the first owner (the one who performed istilad). Then it explains the guarantee: the first owner must guarantee to his partner half the 'uqr and half the value of the slave girl. This is because upon her 'ajz, it becomes clear that he actually owned the partner's share from the time of intercourse (istilad), so he must compensate the partner. The passage then presents a new scenario: if one of the co-owners frees the mukataba (i.e., gives her freedom from his share) while he is wealthy, and then she becomes incapable, the freeing owner must guarantee half her value to his partner and has the right to recover that amount from her. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The commentary explains that the child is for the first owner because his paternity claim is valid: his istilad occurred while the kitaba was still in effect, and istilad of a mukataba is valid by consensus. However, in this case, the kitaba does not remain because when the first caused istilad, he owned half of the partner. Consequently, the mudabbir (second owner) no longer has any ownership over her, so his tadbir is invalid. The passage also clarifies that the half-value guarantee is because he owned half of her through istilad, which is ownership by value (i.e., she becomes his property but he must pay the value). The commentator notes that before 'ajz, the emancipator cannot be made to guarantee the partner's share, because emancipation is divisible in Abu Hanifa's view: it makes the non-emancipated share like that of a mukatab, so the partner's share does not change.

Key Terms
الضمان (al-ḍamān): A legal obligation to compensate for loss or value, here the value of the partner's share.
القيمة (al-qīmah): The market value of the slave woman, used to determine compensation when ownership is transferred retroactively.
الرجوع (al-rujūʿ): The right of the guarantor to recover what he paid from the mukataba after she becomes able or upon her 'ajz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if one co-owner frees a mukataba while wealthy and she later becomes unable, what does he have to do?
A) He must pay half the value to his partner but cannot recover it.
B) He must pay half the value to his partner and can recover it from her.
C) He must pay the full value to his partner and free her entirely.
D) He does not need to pay anything because the freedom is void.

Key Takeaway
When a co-owner frees his share of a mukataba and she later becomes incapable, he must compensate the partner with half her value and can recoup that amount from her.

Mudabbir's Options When Co-Owner Frees: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mudabbir's options after co-owner frees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the options available to a mudabbir according to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn when a co-owner frees the shared slave, and identify the underlying principle (divisibility of tadbir) that generates the difference.

Core Idea
The divisibility of tadbir determines whether the mudabbir can free his share after co-owner frees.

When a slave is co-owned, and one owner makes him a mudabbir (promises freedom upon death), then the other owner frees his share, an intra-Hanafi dispute arises over what options the mudabbir has. Abu Hanifa gives him a set of choices based on the divisibility of tadbir, while the Sahibayn restrict him to financial remedies because they consider freeing indivisible. This lesson walks through the two scenarios and the positions of each imam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Scenario 1: tadbir first (A makes slave mudabbir, then B frees). Scenario 2: itq first (B frees, then A makes slave mudabbir). Note which comes first.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position for each scenario
In Scenario 1, the mudabbir has three options: free the slave, seek earnings (istis'a), or guarantee the freeing partner (tadmeen). In Scenario 2, only two options: free or istis'a; no tadmeen.
3
State the Sahibayn's position for both scenarios
In both scenarios, the mudabbir only has a claim for compensation: tadmeen if the slave is solvent, istis'a if insolvent. No option to free.
Reason: they view freeing (itq) as indivisible; once one owner frees, the slave is entirely free.
4
Identify the underlying principle
Abu Hanifa holds that tadbir is divisible (yatajazza), so the mudabbir's right to free his share survives the co-owner's freeing. Sahibayn hold that itq is indivisible, cancelling the mudabbir's right to free.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa the mudabbir can free and earn reward; under Sahibayn the slave is already free and the mudabbir only gets financial compensation.

(عبد لرجلين) دبرَهُ أحدهما، ثُمَّ حرّره الآخر مليئاً أو عكساً: أي حرره أحدهما، ثُمَّ دبَّرَهُ الآخر، أعتق المدبر، واستسعى فيهما : أي في المسألتين، (أو ضمن شريكه في الأولى فقط، اعلم أنَّ في المسألة الأُولى إذا دبَّرَهُ الأَوَّل، فللثاني الإعتاق، أو التضمين، أو الاستسعاء عند أبي حنيفة، فإذا أعتق الثاني لم يبقَ له ولاية التضمين" والاستسعاء وعندهما: لما كان لا يتجزء بعتق الكلِّ فله أن يضمنه قيمته نصيبه مكاتباً إن كان موسراً، ويستسعي العبد إن كان معسراً ؛ لأنه ضمان إعتاق، فيختلف باليسار والإعسار. كذا في «الهداية»(۱).

ثُمَّ بالاعتاق أفسد نصيب المدبر، فله أن يُعتق، أو يستسعي، أو يضمن قيمته مُدبَّراً، وقد مرَّ في «باب عتق البعض» من «كتاب الإعتاق»: أن قيمة المدبر ثلثا قيمة القن، وإن ضمنه لا يتملكه؛ لأنه لا ينتقل من ملك إلى ملك.

Chapter of the Mukatab: his transactions, incapacity, and rulings. [Page 43] A slave belonging to two men, one of whom made him a mudabbir (gave tadbir), then the other freed him while solvent, or the reverse: the mudabbir may free, or seek earnings from the slave in both cases, or guarantee his partner only in the first case. This according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he does not have recourse (against the other co-owner). This is based on the principle that the silent one, if he makes the freeing partner liable, has recourse against him according to Abu Hanifa, not according to the two. (A slave belonging to two men) one of whom made him a mudabbir, then the other freed him while solvent, or the reverse: i.e., one freed him, then the other made him a mudabbir. The mudabbir may free, or seek earnings in both cases (i.e., in both scenarios), or guarantee his partner only in the first case. Know that in the first scenario, if the first made him a mudabbir, then the second has the options of freeing, or demanding guarantee, or seeking earnings according to Abu Hanifa. So if the second freed, he no longer has authority over guarantee and seeking earnings. According to the two: since the freeing is indivisible, he may guarantee the value of his share as a mukatab if the slave is solvent, and seek earnings from the slave if he is insolvent; because it is a guarantee of freeing, so it varies with solvency and insolvency. Thus in al-Hidayah. [1] His saying: 'This according to Abu Hanifa; and according to the two he does not have recourse'; because when she (the female slave) becomes incapable and returns to slavery, it is as if she never entered into the matter, based on the disagreement about recourse. Regarding the options, according to Abu Hanifa the silent one is given choice among three: if he wishes, he frees; if he wishes, he seeks earnings from the slave; if he wishes, he guarantees his partner the value of his share. According to the two, he only has the guarantee if the slave is solvent, and seeking earnings if insolvent. [2] His saying: 'A slave belonging to two men…' i.e., if the slave is between two men, one made him a mudabbir, then the other freed him while solvent, then the one who made him a mudabbir may, if he wishes, guarantee the freeing partner half the value as a mudabbar, or seek earnings from the slave, or free. If one freed him then the other made him a mudabbir, then the one who made him a mudabbir may, if he wishes, seek earnings from the slave or free, and he does not have the option to guarantee the freeing partner, as in the first scenario. This according to Abu Hanifa, based on the principle that tadbir is divisible according to him. [3] His saying: 'He no longer has authority over guarantee…' because the effect of guarantee is that the guarantor owns the portion of the one guaranteed against, and the freeing partner does not own. Thus in some glosses of al-Hidayah. [Page 44] Then, by the freeing, he spoiled the share of the mudabbir, so he may free, or seek earnings, or guarantee his value as a mudabbar. It has passed in 'Chapter of Freeing a Part' from 'Book of Freeing' that the value of a mudabbar is two-thirds the value of a qinn (non-mudabbar slave). And if he guarantees him, he does not own him; because it does not transfer from ownership to ownership. [1] His saying: 'He spoiled…' because before his freeing, he had the right of service and exploitation, and he was not compelled to hand him over to the freeing partner. After the freeing partner frees, the mudabbir is compelled to hand him over to the freeing partner, and he becomes like a mukatab, and is specifically for the acquiring of earnings. Thus in al-Kifayah. (2) His saying: 'Two-thirds of the value of a qinn' because the benefits are three types: 1. Sale and similar (outside ownership like gift, charity, inheritance, bequest). 2. Service and similar (utilization of benefits like lease, loan, intercourse, freeing, and their concomitants like kitaba). 3. Istilad, tadbir, and freeing for compensation. What is lost in the mudabbar is the first type, i.e., sale, so one-third is deducted. Thus in some glosses of al-Hidayah. [3] His saying: 'And if he guarantees him, he does not own him' i.e., by the guarantee; because this is a guarantee of prevention (between owner and owned), not a guarantee of ownership. Indeed, by the freeing he removed service and other rights from the master, so he becomes like a usurper. Thus in al-Kifayah. [4] His saying: 'It does not transfer from ownership to ownership' as when a mudabbar is usurped and the usurper guarantees the value, he does not own it; and if the usurper finds him, he may seek earnings from him. Thus in al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage from the Book of the Mukatab deals with a co-owned slave where one owner is a mudabbir (has given tadbir, i.e., promised freedom upon his death) and the other owner frees his share. The text presents the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on the options available to the mudabbir after the other owner frees the slave. The passage clarifies two scenarios: (1) tadbir first then itq (freeing) by the other, and (2) itq first then tadbir. According to Abu Hanifa, in the first scenario the mudabbir has three choices: he may free the slave, or seek earnings (istis'a) from the slave (i.e., make him work to pay off the value), or guarantee (tadmeen) his partner (i.e., make the freeing partner pay him the value of his share). In the second scenario (itq first then tadbir), Abu Hanifa says the mudabbir does not have the option to guarantee the freeing partner; he may only free or seek earnings. The reason for this difference is that tadbir is divisible according to Abu Hanifa, so after the other owner frees his share, the mudabbir's tadbir remains on his share. However, the freeing partner's act does not create ownership in the mudabbir's share, so the mudabbir cannot guarantee him. The Sahibayn disagree: they hold that in both scenarios the mudabbir does not have a complete choice. According to them, since the freeing is indivisible (the slave becomes entirely free if one owner frees his share, provided he is solvent), the mudabbir can only demand that the freeing partner pay him the value of his share as a mukatab (if the slave is solvent) or seek earnings from the slave (if insolvent). They do not allow the mudabbir to free the slave himself because the freeing has already been done by the other. The passage then discusses the value of a mudabbar slave, which is two-thirds of a regular slave (qinn) because the right of sale is lost. Finally, it explains that when the mudabbir guarantees the value, he does not own the slave; it is a guarantee of prevention, not a transfer of ownership. This is illustrated by the analogy of a usurped mudabbar. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) for today is not explicitly given here, but the passage indicates that Abu Hanifa's view is the one elaborated upon in al-Hidayah and its commentaries. The key takeaway for students is to understand the differing principles: Abu Hanifa upholds the divisibility of tadbir and gives the mudabbir full options, while the Sahibayn consider the freeing indivisible and restrict the mudabbir's recourse to financial compensation depending on the slave's solvency.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): A contract by which a master promises his slave freedom upon the master's death; it is a suspended emancipation (ta'liq al-itq bi al-mawt).
استسعاء (istis'a): The act of requiring a slave to work to earn money to pay off his value to the master; here it is an option for the mudabbir to make the slave earn his share's value.
تضمين (tadmeen): Holding the freeing partner liable for the value of the mudabbir's share; a claim for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, how many options does the mudabbir have when the other co-owner frees the slave first and then he (the mudabbir) gives tadbir?
A) Three: free, istis'a, tadmeen
B) Two: free and istis'a
C) Two: tadmeen and istis'a
D) One: only tadmeen

Key Takeaway
The divisibility of tadbir under Abu Hanifa gives the mudabbir more options compared to the Sahibayn's view that freeing is indivisible.

intermediate

Co-owner's Options When One Frees and One Makes Mudabbar
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sequence of i'taq and tadbir by co-owners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the options of a co-owner who makes a slave mudabbar after the other co-owner frees his share, and apply the different rulings to determine the correct guarantee.

Core Idea
Tadbir divides according to Abu Hanifa but not according to the Sahibayn—this changes the guarantor's options and compensation.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya delves into the subtle intra-Hanafi disagreement on the sequence of tadbir and i'taq by co-owners. The ruling hinges on whether tadbir is considered divisible among co-owners or an indivisible act. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to predict the outcome when co-owners make conflicting choices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two sequences: one co-owner makes tadbir then the other frees, and one frees then the other makes tadbir. Distinguish them.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position for each scenario
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the second co-owner has three options: free, seek earnings, or guarantee. However, in the second scenario (i'taq then tadbir), choosing tadbir forfeits the right to guarantee.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn hold that both tadbir and i'taq are indivisible. Therefore, if one makes tadbir, the other's freeing is void; if one frees, the other's tadbir is void. Compensation differs based on wealth.
4
Compare the type of guarantee
Abu Hanifa's guarantee is daman hiyalah (barring) not tamlik (ownership) in the first scenario; the Sahibayn's guarantee in the second scenario is daman tamlik (ownership) for the first co-owner's act.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Given a specific fact pattern (e.g., Zaid frees, Amr makes tadbir), predict the outcome according to each view and explain the practical difference.

Then by freeing he corrupts the share of the mudabbar, so he may free, or seek earnings (tas'iyyah), or guarantee its value as a mudabbar. And it has passed in 'Chapter of freeing part' from 'Book of Emancipation' that the value of a mudabbar is two-thirds the value of a free slave. And if he guarantees it, he does not own it; because it does not transfer from ownership to ownership. The share of the one guaranteeing, and the one freeing does not own. Thus in some marginal notes of al-Hidayah. [1] His statement: 'corrupts...' etc.; because before his freeing he owned the use and exploitation, and was not forced to give up to the one freeing, but after the freeing of the one freeing, the mudabbar becomes forced to give up to the one freeing, and becomes like a mukatab, and is specialized to his earnings. Thus in al-Kifayah. (2) His statement: 'two-thirds the value of a free slave'; because benefits are three types: 1. Sale and its likes in being outside ownership like gift, charity, inheritance, bequest. 2. Use and its examples in being utilization of benefits like lease, loan, intercourse, freeing and its appendages from kitabah. And istilad, tadbir, and freeing for compensation. And what is lost in the mudabbar is the first type, i.e., sale, so one-third falls. Thus in some marginal notes of al-Hidayah. [3] His statement: 'And if he guarantees it he does not own it'; i.e., by guarantee; because this is a guarantee of barring between owner and owned, not a guarantee of ownership, for by freeing he removed use and other things from the master, so he becomes like a usurper. Thus in al-Kifayah. [4] His statement: 'does not transfer from ownership to ownership'; as if he usurped a mudabbar and it escaped so the usurper guaranteed the value, the usurper does not own it, and if the usurper finds it he may seek earnings (tas'iyyah). Thus in al-Kifayah. And as for the second issue: If the first frees, then the other has the three options according to him (Abu Hanifa). So if he makes it mudabbar, he no longer has the authority of guarantee, but has the authority of freeing or seeking earnings. So the authority of freeing or seeking earnings is established in both issues, and guarantee is specific to the first. And according to them (the two companions): If one of them makes it mudabbar, then the freeing of the other is void; because tadbir does not divide according to them, so he owns the share of his companion by tadbir, and guarantees half its value as a free slave, whether the slave is wealthy or poor; because it is a guarantee of ownership, so it does not differ with wealth and poverty. And if one of them frees it, then the tadbir of the other is void; because freeing does not divide according to them, so he guarantees half its value if the slave is wealthy, and the slave seeks earnings (tas'iyyah) if poor; because this is a guarantee of freeing, so it differs with wealth and poverty. [1] His statement: 'he no longer has...' etc.; because by initiating tadbir he becomes absolving the one freeing from that his share was a free slave at the time of the freeing of the one freeing, so his guarantee of him was conditional on making the property owned by the guarantee, and he had eliminated that by tadbir, unlike the first, for there his share was a mudabbar at that time, so guarantee is not conditional on making the property owned from him. Thus in al-Inayah. [2] His statement: 'does not differ with wealth and poverty'; and he guarantees half its value as a free slave; because tadbir encountered him while he was a free slave. Thus in al-Hidayah. [3] His statement: 'does not divide according to them'; so he frees all of him, so tadbir does not encounter ownership, and he relies on it so he guarantees... etc.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage addresses the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the rights of two co-owners of a slave when one frees his share and the other responds by making the slave a mudabbar (tadbir), or vice versa. The text distinguishes between two scenarios. In the first scenario (page 44), the second co-owner makes the slave a mudabbar after the first has freed his share. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the second co-owner has three options: free his share (i'taq), seek compensation from the slave (tas'iyyah), or guarantee the value of the slave as a mudabbar (daman). However, the text notes that if he chooses to guarantee, he does not own the slave; it is a guarantee of barring (daman hiyalah) not ownership, because the freed portion does not transfer ownership. The value of a mudabbar is two-thirds of a normal slave because one of three benefit types (sale) is lost. In the second scenario (page 45), if the first co-owner frees his share and the second makes the slave a mudabbar, Imam Abu Hanifa again gives the second the three options, but the right to guarantee is lost if he chooses tadbir. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that tadbir does not divide; hence, if one co-owner makes the slave mudabbar, he owns the other's share and must pay half its value as a free slave regardless of the slave's financial status (because it is a guarantee of ownership). Conversely, if one frees his share, the other's tadbir is void, and he must pay half the value if the slave is wealthy, or the slave must work (tas'iyyah) if poor (because it is a guarantee of freeing, which varies with ability). The text then explains the reasoning: In the first scenario (where the second makes tadbir after the first's i'taq), the second's guarantee was conditional on acquiring ownership through the guarantee, but by making tadbir he forfeits that condition. In the second scenario (where the first frees and the second makes tadbir), the first's share was already mudabbar when the second acted, so no such condition applies.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): Master's declaration that a slave will be free upon the master's death.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee of compensation for the value of the slave; here distinguished between daman hiyalah (barring) and daman tamlik (ownership).
استسعاء (tas'iyyah): Seeking earnings from the slave to compensate the co-owner for his share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what happens if one co-owner makes a slave mudabbar and then the other co-owner frees his share?
A) The freeing is valid and the mudabbar status is void.
B) The freeing is void and the mudabbar owner must pay half the value.
C) Both actions are valid and the slave is half-free, half-mudabbar.
D) The slave is fully freed and the mudabbar owner forfeits his rights.

Key Takeaway
The divisibility of tadbir versus i'taq determines whether the second co-owner can still exercise his options or must compensate, with the Sahibayn treating both as indivisible.

Co-Owned Slave: Tadbir and Itq – Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on co-owned slave remedies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on a co-owned slave when one owner makes him mudabbar or frees him, and apply the resulting options or compensation.

Core Idea
Tadbir is indivisible for the Sahibayn but partial for Abu Hanifa – altering the second owner's remedies.

Ownership of a slave can be shared, and each co-owner may wish to free or declare tadbir. But what happens when one owner acts first? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree on whether the second owner's options change based on the first owner's choice. This lesson walks through the ruling and the reasoning behind each view.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
We have a slave owned by two persons. One owner (say A) either frees the slave or makes him mudabbar (free upon A's death). What are the rights of the other owner (B)?
The slave is not yet free at the moment of tadbir – freedom is deferred until the master's death.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa: If A frees, B has three options: free, make mudabbar, or claim compensation. If A makes mudabbar, B loses the compensation option but retains the options to free or seek service (istis'a').
The reasoning is that tadbir is a partial transfer; B's share is not fully transferred to A, so B still has some authority.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position
The two disciples hold that both tadbir and itq are indivisible. If A makes mudabbar, B's freeing is void; B must pay half the slave's value regardless of his own wealth. If A frees, B's tadbir is void; B pays half if wealthy, else the slave works for compensation (istis'a').
They treat tadbir like itq – it transfers full ownership of the slave to the acting owner, so the other's act is ineffective.
4
Analyze the key difference
The dispute hinges on whether tadbir (and itq) can be partial. Abu Hanifa allows partial effectiveness; the Sahibayn require indivisibility. This affects the second owner's remedies.
Practical consequence: Under Abu Hanifa, B can still free the mudabbar slave; under the Sahibayn, B cannot and must pay compensation.
5
Locate the reasoning in the commentary
The footnotes from al-'Inayah and al-Hidayah explain: Abu Hanifa sees tadbir as a kind of release from liability; the Sahibayn see it as a transfer of ownership. Understand these foundational concepts.
Al-'Inayah: making tadbir exonerates the other from liability. Al-Hidayah: tadbir encounters the slave as qinn (owned), so compensation is due.

Source Text

As for the second issue: If the first [owner] frees [the slave], then the other has the three options according to him [Abu Hanifa]. If he makes him mudabbar, he no longer has the authority of compensation, but he retains the authority of freeing or seeking service. So the authority of freeing or seeking service is established in both issues, and compensation pertains only to the first [issue]. According to them [the two disciples]: If one of them makes him mudabbar, then the freeing by the other is void, because tadbir is indivisible according to them. So he owns the share of his companion by tadbir, and he guarantees half its value as a slave, whether [the other] is wealthy or poor, because it is a guarantee of ownership, so it does not differ based on wealth or poverty. And if one of them frees him, then the tadbir of the other is void, because freeing is indivisible according to them. So he guarantees half its value if [the other] is wealthy, and the slave works for wages if [the other] is poor, because this is a guarantee of freeing, so it differs based on wealth and poverty.
[1] His saying: 'He no longer has...' etc.; because by directly making mudabbar, he becomes an exonerator of the freed one from [the fact that] his share was a slave at the time of the freeing by the freed one. So his compensation of him was conditional upon transferring ownership of the property by guarantee, and he forfeited that by tadbir, unlike the first [issue], because there his share was mudabbar at that time, so the compensation is not conditional upon transferring ownership from him. As in al-'Inayah.
[2] His saying: 'It does not differ based on wealth and poverty'; he guarantees half its value as a slave because tadbir encountered him while he was a slave. As in al-Hidayah.
[3] His saying: 'It is indivisible according to them'; so the entire [slave] becomes free, so tadbir did not encounter ownership, and it relies on it, so he guarantees... etc.

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a slave owned by two persons when one owner either frees the slave or makes him mudabbar (free upon death). The text presents the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and then the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). According to Abu Hanifa, if the first owner frees the slave, the second owner has three options: free, make mudabbar, or claim compensation. If the first owner makes him mudabbar, the second loses the option of compensation and retains only the options of freeing or seeking service. According to the Sahibayn, both tadbir and itq are indivisible: if one owner makes the slave mudabbar, the other's freeing is void, and he must pay half the slave's value regardless of his own wealth. If one frees, the other's tadbir is void, and he pays half if wealthy; otherwise the slave works to compensate. The footnotes clarify the reasoning: in Abu Hanifa's view, making mudabbar effectively releases the other from liability; in the Sahibayn's view, the act of tadbir or itq transfers full ownership, requiring compensation.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): A contract where a master declares that his slave will be free upon the master's death.
تضمين (tadmin): Claim for compensation, specifically the value of the slave's share.
استسعاء (istis'a'): Requiring the slave to work to earn his freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The freeing is valid and the other owner is entitled to compensation.
B) The freeing is void and the other owner must pay half the slave's value regardless of his wealth.
C) The freeing is void and the other owner must pay half only if he is wealthy.
D) The freeing is valid and the other owner has no claim.

Key Takeaway
The core issue is whether tadbir is divisible; Abu Hanifa allows options, the Sahibayn require compensation.

Judge's Procedure When Mukatab Defaults
Judge's procedure on default of mukatab installment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the judge's procedure when a mukatab fails to pay an installment, applying the condition of whether the mukatab has a means to obtain the funds.

Core Idea
A mukatab who cannot pay an installment gets three days' grace if he has a way to get money; otherwise the judge voids the contract immediately.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Hanafi fiqh on the kitabah contract. When a mukatab (a slave working for freedom) fails to pay an installment, the judge must assess his ability to pay in the near future. The ruling balances the rights of the master with the possibility of the slave obtaining funds, granting a brief grace period if there is hope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the mukatab's failure to pay a star (installment). The ruling is the judge's response: either declare ta'jiz (incapacity) immediately or after a three-day delay.
2
Determine the condition for grace
The critical condition is whether the mukatab has a 'wajh' (means) that will reach him, such as a collectable debt or distant property expected soon.
If no such means, the judge declares ta'jiz immediately.
3
Apply the rule
If a means exists, the judge gives three days' grace. If after three days the mukatab still hasn't paid, the judge declares ta'jiz and the kitabah is rescinded. If no means, immediate ta'jiz.
The three-day period is standard for granting excuses in Hanafi fiqh.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

مكاتب عجز عن نجم إن كان له وجه سيصل إليه لا يعجزه الحاكم إلى ثلاثة أيام والا عجزه

Chapter of Death and Incapacity
A mukatab who is incapable [of paying] a star: if he has a means that will reach him, the judge does not declare him incapacitated until three days; otherwise he declares him incapacitated.
Chapter of Death and Incapacity
[T]
(A mukatab who is incapable of [paying] a star: if he has a means that will reach him, the judge does not declare him incapacitated until three days) i.e., if three days pass and he does not pay the portion of that star, he rules his incapacity; (otherwise he declares him incapacitated) i.e., if he does not have a means that will reach him, he declares him incapacitated.
[1] His saying: Chapter of death and incapacity; i.e., the death of the mukatab and his inability to pay the kitabah compensation, and the death of the master. The postponement of the rulings of these matters is clearly appropriate because these matters are subsequent to the kitabah contract; similarly, the explanation of their rulings. As in al-Nata'ij.
[2] His saying: 'About a star'; the star is the ascending [sign], then it is used for the fixed time, then for the installment to be paid at that time. As in al-'Inayah.
[3] His saying: 'If he has a means'; i.e., if he has a debt he can collect, or absent property whose arrival is hoped for. Al-ta'jiz means attribution to incapacity and ruling accordingly; al-hakim is the judge. As in Hashiyat al-Jalabi, quoting from al-Bayanīyah.
[4] His saying: 'Until three days'; because three is the period set for granting excuses, such as giving the opposing party time to pay, or giving the debtor time to fulfill; so it is not increased beyond that. As in al-Hidayah.
[5] His saying: 'Means'; i.e., the mukatab has a way to obtain money and the master demands his incapacity from the judge; the judge declares him incapacitated and rescinds the kitabah.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on the death or incapacity of a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission for a payment). The ruling states: if a mukatab fails to pay an installment (star), the judge looks at his circumstances. If he has a means to obtain the money—such as a debt he can collect or distant property that will come to him—the judge does not immediately declare him incapacitated; instead, he gives him a grace period of three days. If after three days he still hasn't paid, the judge declares him incapacitated, which voids the kitabah contract and the slave remains a slave. If the mukatab has no such means, the judge declares him incapacitated immediately. The commentary explains that 'star' originally means an ascendant, then the fixed time, then the installment. The three-day period is standard for granting excuses, similar to other fiqh contexts. The judge's role is to assess whether the mukatab can realistically pay in the near future.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has a contract (kitabah) with his master to earn his freedom by paying a specified sum in installments.
نجم (najm): An installment of the kitabah payment, due at a fixed time.
عجز (ajz): Inability of the mukatab to pay the installment, leading to potential rescission of the contract.
تعجيز (ta'jiz): The judge's declaration that the mukatab is incapable, resulting in the cancellation of the kitabah contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the judge do if a mukatab defaults on a star but has a debt that he can collect within a short time?
A) Immediately declares him incapacitated.
B) Gives him a three-day grace period to collect the debt.
C) Orders the master to reduce the installment.
D) Requires the mukatab to work for the master until the debt is paid.

Key Takeaway
A mukatab's default does not automatically invalidate the contract; the judge must consider if he can pay within three days.
