Three-Day Grace for Mukatab Who Can Pay
Ruling on deferring declaration of incapacity for a mukatab with prospective income

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mukatab's missed installment with a prospective source of income and apply the qiyas that justifies the three-day grace period.

Core Idea
If a mukatab has foreseeable income, the judge must wait three days before declaring incapacity.

This passage from al-Hidayah explains a specific mercy built into the kitabah contract: when a mukatab misses an installment but has a reasonable prospect of paying soon, the judge does not immediately rescind the contract. Instead, a three-day grace period is granted, modelled on analogous delays in other legal matters. Understanding this ruling highlights the Hanafi commitment to facilitating eventual freedom through purposeful opportunity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon and its legal classification. Here, the act is the judge's declaration of incapacity (ta'jiz) for a mukatab who missed an installment. The ruling is that the judge must delay such a declaration for three days if the mukatab has a foreseeable source of income.
The ruling is wajib on the judge.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof that supports this ruling. The proof is a qiyas (analogy) to the three-day grace period given to debtors and defendants in other legal contexts, as explicitly mentioned in the commentary: 'because three is the period set for granting excuses, such as giving the opponent time to respond and the debtor time to pay'.
The dalil is a rational proof (qiyas jaliy).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the analogy operates. The common effective cause (illa) is the need to grant an opportunity for fulfilling an obligation before penalizing non-compliance. This illa is present both for a debtor who needs time to pay and for a mukatab who has a prospective income source.
The illa is derived from tahrir al-manat: the shared rationale of enabling performance.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify any scholarly disagreement. The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih; other schools may have different rules (e.g., some may require immediate ta'jiz or different grace periods). The Hanafi position relies on this qiyas.
The ikhtilaf is legitimate and based on differing usul.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Determine which position is relied upon. The ruling is mufta bihi from the Zahir al-Riwayah, with no intra-Hanafi dispute on this specific point. The three-day grace is firmly established.
Tarjih authority is the weight of Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text

باب الموت والعجز
مكاتب عجز عن نجم إن كان له وجه سيصل إليه لا يعجزه الحاكم إلى ثلاثة أيام والا عجزه
قوله: باب الموت والعجز؛ أي موت المكاتب وعجزه عن أداء بدل الكتابة، وموت المولى، تأخير أحكام هذه الأشياء ظاهر التناسب لأن هذه الأشياء متأخرة عن عقد الكتابة، فكذا بيان أحكامها. كذا في «النتائج».
(٢) قوله: عن نجم؛ النجم هو الطالع، ثم سمي به الوقت المضروب، ثم سمي ما يؤدي فيه من الوظيفة. كذا في «العناية».
[٣] قوله: إن كان له وجه؛ بأن كان له دين يقبضه، أو مال غائب يرجى قدومه، والتعجيز: النسبة إلى العجز والحكم به، والحاكم القاضي. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي ناقلاً عن «البيانية».
[٤] قوله: إلى ثلاثة أيام؛ لأن الثلاث هي المدة التي ضربت لإيلاء الأعذار، كإمهال الخصم للدفع، وإمهال المديون للقضاء، فلا يزداد عليه. كذا في «الهداية».
[0]قوله: وجه؛ أي للمكاتب وجه لتحصيل المال وطلب المولى بعجزه عن الحاكم عجزه الحاكم، وفسخ الكتابة.
Chapter of death and incapacity. A mukatab who fails to pay an installment: if he has a source of income that will reach him, the judge does not declare him incapacitated for three days; otherwise, the judge declares him incapacitated. [Footnote 1:] His saying: Chapter of death and incapacity; that is, the death of the mukatab and his incapacity to pay the kitabah price, and the death of the master. The delay in the rulings of these matters is apparent in their suitability because these matters occur after the contract of kitabah; similarly, the explanation of their rulings. Thus in 'al-Nata'ij'. [2] His saying: 'fail to pay an installment'; an-najm originally means the rising (of a star), then was used for the appointed time, then for the amount due at that time. Thus in 'al-Inaya'. [3] His saying: 'if he has a source of income'; such as having a debt he can collect or distant property expected to arrive. At-ta'jiz means ascribing incapacity and ruling by it, and al-hakim is the judge. Thus in 'Hashiyat al-Jalabi' quoting from 'al-Bayaniyya'. [4] His saying: 'for three days'; because three is the period set for granting excuses, such as giving the opponent time to respond and the debtor time to pay, so it should not be increased. Thus in 'al-Hidaya'. [0] His saying: 'source of income'; that is, the mukatab has a way to obtain money, and the master's request for his incapacitation before the judge leads the judge to declare his incapacity and rescind the kitabah.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on a mukatab (a slave under contract of manumission) who fails to pay an installment (najm) of his kitabah price. The judge (hakim) does not immediately declare him incapacitated ('ajz) if the mukatab has a 'face' (wajh) — meaning a foreseeable source of income, such as a debt owed to him or distant property that is expected to arrive. Instead, the judge grants a three-day grace period. If he does not pay within three days, the judge declares him incapable, which leads to rescission (faskh) of the kitabah contract. The commentator explains each term: 'najm' evolved from the rising star to the time of payment to the amount due. The three-day period is analogized to other legal delays in Hanafi fiqh, such as the adjournment given to a defendant or a debtor, to allow for possible payment. This ruling is based on the principle of facilitating the mukatab's freedom, as the objective of kitabah is eventual manumission. The condition of having a 'source' ensures that the delay is purposeful and not simply a postponement.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah with his master, whereby he will pay a sum in installments in exchange for his freedom.
نجم (najm): An installment of the kitabah price, named after the time (like the rising of a star) at which it is due.
وجه (wajh): A foreseeable source of income that the mukatab can use to pay the installment, such as a debt receivable or distant property.
تعجيز (ta'jiz): The act of the judge declaring the mukatab incapable of paying, thereby terminating the kitabah contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what condition must be met for the judge to delay declaring a mukatab incapacitated after he misses an installment?
A) The mukatab must be ill.
B) The mukatab must have a foreseeable source of income to pay the installment.
C) The master must request the delay.
D) The installment must be less than one-third of the total kitabah.

Key Takeaway
The three-day grace for a mukatab with a source of income is wajib, not optional, because the objective of kitabah is eventual freedom and the analogy to debtor grace is sound.

Dispute on Number of Missed Installments for Incapacity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the condition for incapacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Abu Yusuf on the number of missed installments required to declare a mukatab incapacitated, and analyze the proofs for each view.

Core Idea
One missed installment proves incapacity for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; Abu Yusuf requires two consecutive missed installments.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about when a mukatab (slave under a kitabah contract) can be declared incapacitated ('ajz) due to missed payments. The ruling determines whether the master can petition for rescission (faskh) after one or two missed installments. We will explore the reasoning of each imam and the evidence they cite, drawing from al-Hidayah and its commentaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine what specific condition triggers the declaration of incapacity: is it one missed installment or two consecutive missed installments?
The dispute is about the number of installments, not the validity of the kitabah contract itself.
2
State the positions
State clearly the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (one installment suffices) and that of Abu Yusuf (two consecutive missed installments required).
Both views are within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the proofs
Examine the proof for each position: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rely on qiyas (if unable to pay one, even more unable to pay two) and the master's objective; Abu Yusuf relies on the saying of 'Ali (ra) and its implied condition.
Pay attention to the concept of mafhum al-shart (concept of a condition) and its validity in Hanafi usul.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Understand the refutation of Abu Yusuf's proof: the concept of condition is not binding, and conflicting narrations (Ibn Umar) weaken his evidence.
The commentator also notes a nuance: the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad does not apply if only one installment remains after paying all others.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify the relied-upon position for fatwa: the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad prevails, as supported by al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah.
This has practical implications for the master and the judge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وفسخها بطلب سيده، أو سيده برضاه
[١][قوله]: وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد الله؛ لأن سبب الفسخ قد تحقق، وهو العجز؛ لأن من عجز عن أداء نجم واحد يكون أعجز عن أداء نجمين، وهذا أي كون العجز سبباً للفسخ؛ لأن مقصود المولى الوصول إلى المال عند حلول نجم، وقد فات فيفسخ؛ أي المولى أو القاضي؛ لاختلاف الروايتين إذا لم يكن راضياً به دونه. قال أخي وأستاذي مولانا أبو الحسنات عبد الحي نور الله مرقده، أقول: فيه ء، وهو أن دليلهما لا يتمشى في صورة أدى سائر النجوم وبقي نجم واحد معجز. كذا في «نتائج الأفكار». أقول: الاستدلال الشامل من قوله: وهذا وقوله: إن سبب الفسخ ... الخ، دفع في مقابلة استدلال أبي يوسف، والصورة المذكورة لا وجود لتوالي النجمين فيها، فافهم. انتهى. بخلاف اليومين والثلاثة؛ لأنه لا بد منها لإمكان الأداء، فلم يكن تأخيراً، والآثار متعارضة، فإن المروي عن ابن عمر أن مكاتبة له عجزت عن نجم فردها، فسقط الاحتجاج بها. كذا في «الهداية» وحواشيه.
[٢]قوله: وعند أبي يوسف الله لا يعجزه؛ أي لا يحكم الحاكم بعجزه سواء كان له وجه سيصل أو لم يكن، حتى يتعاقب على المكاتب نجمان؛ لقول علي: إذا توالى على المكاتب نجمان رد في الرق. علقه بهذا الشرط فلا يرد بدونه، ولقائل أن يقول: هذا استدلال بمفهوم الشرط، وهو ليس بقائم؛ لأنه يفيد الوجود عند الوجود فقط.
(۱) أي فسخ الحاكم الكتابة بعد عجز المكاتب بطلب مولاه. ينظر: «درر الحکام»(۲: ۳۱).
Book of the Mukatab: His Transactions, Incapacity, and Rulings. And its rescission at the request of his master, or the master [requests] by his [the mukatab's] consent. [Footnote 1:] His saying: And this is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (peace be upon them) because the cause of rescission has been realized, which is incapacity; because one who fails to pay a single installment is more incapable of paying two installments. And this—that is, incapacity being a cause for rescission—is because the master's objective is to receive the money when the installment is due, and it has been missed, so he rescinds—i.e., the master or the judge, based on two different transmissions, if he is not satisfied without it. My brother and teacher, our master Abu al-Hasanat 'Abd al-Hayy (may Allah illuminate his resting place), said: I say: There is a point here: that their proof does not apply in the case where he paid all other installments and only one remains unpaid. Thus in 'Nata'ij al-Afkar'. I say: The comprehensive inference from his statement 'and this' and his statement 'the cause of rescission' etc. is a refutation in response to Abu Yusuf's inference, and the mentioned scenario does not involve two consecutive installments, so understand. End. Contrary to the two or three days [grace] because they are necessary to enable payment, so it is not a delay, and the narrations are conflicting, for it is narrated from Ibn 'Umar that a mukataba of his failed to pay an installment, and he returned her [to slavery]; thus the argument [of Abu Yusuf] falls. Thus in 'al-Hidaya' and its glosses. [2] His saying: And according to Abu Yusuf (may Allah be pleased with him) he does not declare him incapacitated—i.e., the judge does not rule his incapacity, whether he has a source of income or not, until two installments have passed over the mukatab consecutively; due to the saying of 'Ali: 'When two installments come consecutively upon a mukatab, he is returned to slavery.' He attached it to this condition, so he is not returned without it. And it can be said: This is inference by the concept of a condition, which is not valid because it only indicates existence when the condition exists.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute over the condition for judicially declaring a mukatab incapable ('ajz) due to missed payments. The matn states that the rescission (faskh) of the kitabah contract occurs at the request of the master, or with the mukatab's consent. The commentator then explains that according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, missing a single installment is sufficient to establish incapacity, because the cause of rescission—inability to pay—is realized; one who cannot pay one installment is even less able to pay two. The master's objective is to receive payment at the due time, and if that objective is defeated, rescission is justified. The commentator, 'Abd al-Hayy al-Lakhnawi, notes a potential flaw: the reasoning does not apply if the mukatab has paid all previous installments and only one remains, but he clarifies that Abu Hanifa's argument still holds because consecutive installments are not required. On the other hand, Abu Yusuf holds that the judge cannot declare incapacity until two consecutive installments are missed. He relies on the statement of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'When two installments come consecutively upon a mukatab, he is returned to slavery.' The commentator refutes Abu Yusuf's use of this statement, arguing that it is an inference from the concept of a condition, which only proves existence when the condition exists, not the converse. Furthermore, there is a conflicting narration from Ibn 'Umar who returned a mukataba after one missed installment. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it aligns with the broader objective of the contract.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of the kitabah contract, resulting in the mukatab returning to full slavery.
توالى (tawala): To come consecutively; used of two installments in succession.
مفهوم الشرط (mafhum al-shart): The inferred meaning from a conditional statement, meaning that the ruling applies only when the condition is met (concept of condition).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, how many missed installments are required to declare a mukatab incapacitated?
A) One missed installment.
B) Two consecutive missed installments.
C) Three missed installments.
D) Any number as long as the master is dissatisfied.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon ruling is that a mukatab is considered incapacitated after missing a single installment, allowing the master to seek rescission of the kitabah contract.

Incapacity of the Mukatab and Rescission of Kitabah
Explains ruling on incapacity and rescission with Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on when a mukatab is considered incapacitated, explain Abu Yusuf's dissenting view, and identify the evidence used by each side, including the hadith of Ali.

Core Idea
One missed installment can trigger rescission of kitabah, but Abu Yusuf insists on two consecutive missed installments.

This lesson examines the legal conditions under which a mukatab (a slave under a kitabah contract to purchase freedom) is considered incapacitated (ajz), leading to the rescission (faskh) of the contract. We will explore the majority Hanafi view of Abu Hanifah and Muhammad al-Shaybani, who hold that missing a single installment is sufficient, and the dissenting opinion of Abu Yusuf, who requires two consecutive missed installments. The lesson also covers the procedural requirements for rescission and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
The subject is the kitabah contract and its rescission due to the mukatab's incapacity. The ruling is that rescission is valid when incapacity is established.
The overall hukm is that the rescission itself is a valid legal action (sahih).
2
State the majority position
According to Abu Hanifah and Muhammad, a mukatab is considered incapacitated if he fails to pay even a single installment, because one who cannot pay one is even less able to pay two, and the master's objective of timely payment is defeated.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
State the dissenting position
Abu Yusuf holds that incapacity is only established when two installments become due consecutively without payment, based on a hadith of Ali that explicitly conditions return to slavery on that.
Abu Yusuf relies on the literal wording of the hadith.
4
Analyze the evidences
The majority argue that the hadith's conditional does not negate the ruling in its absence; it only affirms existence when the condition is met. They also cite a rational analogy and a report from Ibn Umar.
The rebuttal uses the principle that mafhum al-shart is not binding when it conflicts with a stronger evidence.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Practically, under Abu Yusuf's view, the master must wait for two missed installments before rescinding, even if the mukatab is clearly unable to pay. The mufta bihi is the majority view, allowing immediate rescission upon one missed installment.
The tarjih is based on the purpose of the contract and the stronger proof.

(۳) ناقلا عن «التبيين». في «حاشية الجلبي» [٢] قوله : وعاد رقه ؛ أي إذا عجز المكاتب عاد إلى أحكام الرق؛ لانفساخ عقد الكتابة.
[۳] قوله : وما في يده ... الخ ؛ أي ما كان في يده من الإكساب فهو لسيده ؛ لأنه ظهر بالعجز أنه كسب عبده ، وهذا لأنه كان موقوفاً عليه، أو على عبده ؛ لأنه أدّى بدل الكتابة وكسبه للمكاتب، وإن عجز فللمولى فقد زال التوقف بالعجز. كذا في «الكفاية»(4).
Book of the Mukatab: his transactions, incapacity, and rulings. And its rescission at the request of his master, or by his master with his consent. [1] This is according to Abu Hanifah and Muhammad, but according to Abu Yusuf, he is not rendered incapacitated until two installments become due upon him consecutively. And its rescission at the request of his master, or by his master with his consent. [1][His saying]: This is according to Abu Hanifah and Muhammad, because the cause of rescission has been realized, which is incapacity; because whoever is incapable of paying one installment is more incapable of paying two installments. And this is that incapacity is a cause for rescission because the master's objective is to receive the money at the time an installment falls due, and it has failed. So he rescinds—i.e., the master or the judge—due to differing narrations if he is not satisfied without him. My brother and teacher, our master Abu al-Hasanat Abd al-Hayy (may Allah illuminate his resting place), said: I say: there is an issue, which is that their proof does not apply in the case where he paid all other installments and only one installment remains with incapacity. Thus in Nata'ij al-Afkar. Something I say: The comprehensive inference from his saying 'This' and his saying 'the cause of rescission' is a rebuttal to Abu Yusuf's inference, and the mentioned scenario does not involve the succession of two installments, so understand. End. Unlike two or three days, because they are necessary to enable payment, so it is not a delay. And the reports are contradictory, for it is narrated from Ibn Umar that a mukatibah of his was unable to pay an installment and he returned her, so the argument by it is invalid. Thus in al-Hidayah and its glosses. Absolutely. [2] His saying: According to Abu Yusuf, he is not rendered incapacitated—i.e., the judge does not rule his incapacity whether he has a means to pay or not, until two installments become due consecutively upon the mukatab, based on the saying of Ali: 'When two installments become due consecutively upon the mukatab, he is returned to slavery.' He conditioned it with this condition, so he is not returned without it. And someone may say: This is inference from the concept of condition, which is not valid because it only indicates existence upon existence. [PAGE_048] 48 Hasan al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah. And his slavery returns and whatever is in his hand belongs to his master. [1] That is, his master rescinds with the consent of the mukatab, and his slavery returns and whatever is in his hand belongs to his master. And the answer: That which Fakhr al-Islam indicated: it is conditional upon two conditions, and a conditional upon two conditions is not realized by only one, as if he said: 'If you enter these two houses, you are divorced.' Thus in al-Inayah. And because it is a contract of beneficence, so its best form is deferred, and the state of obligation of payment after an installment falls due requires granting a period for seeking ease, and the best period is that agreed upon by the two contracting parties. So when the second installment passes, incapacity is realized, necessitating rescission. Thus in al-Ayni. [1] His saying: with the consent of the mukatab. And it is said: The master alone has the right to rescind, and the consent of the mukatab is not required, just as when the buyer finds a defect in the sold item before taking possession, he alone may rescind; and the hadith of Ibn Umar concerning the mukatibah indicates this. That is because the kitabah is completed by the master's receipt of the payment; so as long as the payment has not been received, it is not completed. So he rescinds independently if his objective is lost, just as the buyer independently rescinds for a defect before possession. We said: The slave after the contract comes into his possession, so this is a rescission after possession, so it requires a court order or consent. Thus transmitted from al-Tabyin in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [2] His saying: And his slavery returns: i.e., when the mukatab becomes incapacitated, he returns to the rulings of slavery, due to the dissolution of the kitabah contract. [3] His saying: And whatever is in his hand, etc.: i.e., whatever earnings were in his hand, it belongs to his master, because it has become clear through his incapacity that it is the earnings of his slave. And this is because it was suspended between him and his slave; because if he pays the kitabah payment, his earnings belong to the mukatab, but if he becomes incapacitated, it belongs to the master; so the suspension is removed by incapacity. Thus in al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah deals with the incapacity of a mukatab (a slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom by installments) and the rescission of the kitabah contract. The author first presents the base ruling: the kitabah can be rescinded at the master's request or with his consent. Then he clarifies the dispute. According to Abu Hanifah and Muhammad al-Shaybani, if the mukatab fails to pay a single installment, he is considered incapacitated, because the cause of rescission—inability to achieve the master's objective of receiving timely payment—is realized. This reasoning applies even if only one installment remains unpaid after others have been paid, as the helper comment notes. Abu Yusuf, however, holds that the mukatab is not deemed incapacitated until two installments have fallen due consecutively without payment, based on a hadith of Ali that explicitly conditions return to slavery on the succession of two installments. The commentator then rebuts Abu Yusuf's use of the conditional concept (mafhum al-shart), arguing that such a conditional only indicates existence when the condition is met, but does not negate the ruling in other cases. The hadith of Ibn Umar (who returned a mukatibah after one missed installment) is also cited but dismissed due to conflicting reports. The passage then explains that rescission by the master requires the mukatab's consent, except according to an alternative view analogizing to the buyer's right to rescind for a defect before delivery. The preferred Hanafi position is that since the slave is in the master's possession after the contract, rescission after that requires either a court order (by a judge) or the slave's consent. Finally, upon rescission, the mukatab returns to full slavery, and whatever earnings he had in his hand go to the master, as the suspension of ownership is resolved by the incapacity.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a kitabah contract with his master to purchase his freedom by paying specified installments.
نجم (najm): An installment or payment term in the kitabah contract.
عجز (ajz): Incapacity to pay the due installment(s), leading to rescission of the contract.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of the kitabah contract, returning the mukatab to full slavery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars (Abu Hanifah and Muhammad), when is a mukatab considered incapacitated such that the kitabah can be rescinded?
A) After missing two consecutive installments.
B) After missing one installment, regardless of the amount remaining.
C) Only if a judge declares him incapacitated after three missed installments.
D) Only if the master agrees to rescind without any missed payment.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the majority (Abu Hanifah and Muhammad) rule that a mukatab's failure to pay one installment establishes incapacity, allowing rescission of the kitabah, while Abu Yusuf requires two consecutive missed installments; the mufta bihi is the majority view.

Ruling on the Mukatab's Incapacity and Its Consequences
Ruling on the effect of the mukatab's incapacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the consequences of a mukatab's incapacity and explain why the master cannot unilaterally dissolve the kitaba contract after possession.

Core Idea
A mukatab's incapacity returns him to slavery and his earnings to the master, but dissolution requires consent or judicial decree because possession has occurred.

When a mukatab (a slave under a kitaba contract) becomes unable to pay his instalments, the contract dissolves. But how does this dissolution happen, and what happens to earnings he already acquired? The Hanafi school clarifies that the master cannot simply declare the contract void on his own; the mukatab's consent or a judge's ruling is needed. This lesson breaks down the ruling and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Read the opening statement: 'And his slavery-status returns and what is in his hand belongs to his master.' This is the core legal effect of the mukatab's incapacity.
2
Understand the alternative view
Note the 'qil' (some scholars hold) that the master can dissolve unilaterally, analogizing to a buyer's right to rescind before possession. Identify the analogy and its source (Ibn Umar's hadith).
This view is rejected in the text.
3
Trace the rebuttal reasoning
The commentary rebuts by distinguishing post-possession: after the kitaba contract, the master has taken possession of the slave's services. Dissolution after possession cannot be unilateral; it requires either the mukatab's consent or a judicial decree.
The key distinction: pre-possession vs. post-possession.
4
Apply the ruling to earnings
If the mukatab paid some instalments but then becomes incapable, his earnings during the kitaba period were suspended. If he succeeds, they are his; if he fails, they revert to the master as earnings of a slave.
This solves the question of who gets the earnings.
5
Evaluate the ikhtilaf
The intra-Hanafi disagreement is legitimate. The mufta bihi position requires consent or judicial decree, as adopted in al-Tabyin and al-`Inayah.
This is the relied-upon fatwa.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on the consequences of a mukatab's incapacity to pay the kitaba instalments. The base text states that upon incapacity, the mukatab returns to being a full slave and his earnings revert to his master. The commentary then clarifies that the dissolution of the contract requires the mukatab's consent; an alternative view allowing unilateral dissolution by the master is rejected. The reasoning is that the kitaba contract is a concession, and the master has already taken possession of the slave's services after the contract, so dissolution after possession requires either the mukatab's consent or a judicial decree. The analogy to a buyer's right to rescind before possession is distinguished because here possession has occurred. The earnings during the kitaba period are considered suspended; if the mukatab pays successfully, they are his, but if he becomes incapable, they become the master's.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of kitaba with his master to earn his freedom by paying a specified sum in instalments.
عجز (cajaza): Incapacity or inability of the mukatab to pay the due instalments of kitaba.
فسخ (faskh): Dissolution; the cancellation of the kitaba contract, returning the mukatab to full slavery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The presence of two witnesses

Key Takeaway
Upon a mukatab's incapacity, his slavery and earnings revert to the master, but the dissolution of the kitaba contract requires consent or judicial decree because possession has already occurred.

The Mukatab's Death with Sufficient Wealth: Hanafi View
Inter-madhhab dispute on survival of kitaba contract after mukatab's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the kitaba survives death, explain the Hanafi proof based on analogy to exchange contracts, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument from Zaid ibn Thabit's hadith.

Core Idea
A mukatab dies free if his estate can pay the kitaba; the contract survives by analogy to any exchange contract.

What happens when a mukatab (a slave working to buy his freedom) dies before completing his payments? Two major schools disagree sharply. The Hanafi school, relying on the opinions of `Ali and Ibn Mas`ud, holds that if his estate has enough money, the contract continues and he dies free. The Shafi'i school argues that death cancels the contract, leaving him a slave. This lesson unpacks the proofs on both sides and shows how the Hanafis use legal analogy (qiyas) and interpretation (ta'wil) to reconcile conflicting evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Ruling
Identify the precise ruling: if a mukatab dies leaving wealth sufficient to pay the remaining kitaba, the contract is not dissolved. The estate pays the debt, and the mukatab is considered free from the time of payment.
This ruling comes from `Ali and Ibn Mas`ud and is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the Shafi'i Position and Its Dalil
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the kitaba is invalidated upon death. His evidence: the hadith of Zaid ibn Thabit: 'The mukatab is a slave as long as a dirham remains upon him, he does not inherit nor is he inherited.' He also argues that the purpose (freedom) cannot be achieved after death.
The hadith is narrated by al-Tirmidhi and is ahad (single-narrator), thus zanni in transmission.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Response: Ta'wil of the Hadith
The Hanafis interpret (ta'wil) the hadith as applying only during life. After death, the ruling changes because the contract can be fulfilled by the estate. The hadith's general wording is restricted to before death.
This ta'wil is justified by the stronger analogy (qiyas) with the master's death, as explained next.
4
Understand the Hanafi Analogy (Qiyas)
The kitaba contract is a contract of exchange (mu'awada). Just as the master's death does not dissolve the contract, the slave's death should not dissolve it either. In fact, the slave's right is stronger: the contract is binding on him, so preservation is more necessary.
The common element (illa) is the need to preserve the contract to protect rights. Death does not negate that need if assets exist.
5
Note the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
If the Hanafi view is followed, the mukatab dies free, his heirs inherit any surplus after paying the kitaba. If the Shafi'i view is followed, he dies a slave, and his master inherits all his property.
This difference has major implications for inheritance and the status of the deceased.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن مات عن وفاء : أي عن مال يفي ببدل الكتابة ، (لم يفسخ ) ، هذا عندنا ، وعند الشافعي (۱) له تبطل الكتابة لفوات المحل، ونحن نقول : [1] قوله : فإن مات عن وفاء ؛ أي إن مات المكاتب عن مال يفي بدل الكتابة لم تفسخ كتابته، هذا عندنا، وهو قول علي وابن مسعود ، وبه نأخذ، وعند الشافعي الله تبطل الكتابة، ويموت عبداً، وما ترك لمولاه، وإمامه في ذلك زيد بن ثابت الله حيث قال : «المكاتب عبد ما بقي عليه درهم لا يرث ولا يورث)، رواه الترمذي. ولأن المقصود من الكتابة عتقه، وقد تعدَّرَ إثباتُهُ بموته فيبطل ؛ وهذا لأنه لا يخلو : إما أن يثبت بعد الموت مقصوداً ، أو يثبت قبله أو بعده مستنداً، لا وجه إلى الأول ؛ لعدم المحلية، ولا إلى الثاني ؛ لفقد الشرط وهو الأداء، ولا إلى الثالث ؛ لتعذر الثبوت في الحال، والشيء يثبتُ في الحال ثم يستند. كذا في «الهداية»(۳). [۲] قوله : ونحن نقول ؛ توضيحهُ : إِنّ عقدَ الكتابة عقد معاوضة، ولا يبطل بموت أحد المتعاقدين، وهو المولى، فكذا لا يبطل بموت الآخر ، وهو العبد، والجامع بينهما الحاجة إلى إبقاء العقد ؛ لإحياء الحق، بل أولى؛ لأن حقه أكد من حق المولى ، حتى لزم العقد في جانب العبد، والموتُ أنفى للمالكية منه للملوكية، فينزل حياً تقديراً. كذا في «الهداية».
If he dies while having sufficient assets: meaning, with wealth that covers the kitaba substitute, (it is not dissolved), this is according to us. And according to al-Shafi'i, the kitaba is invalidated due to the loss of the subject. And we say: [1] His statement: 'If he dies while having sufficient assets'; meaning, if the mukatab dies with wealth that covers the kitaba substitute, his kitaba is not dissolved. This is our view, and it is the saying of `Ali and Ibn Mas`ud, and we adopt it. According to al-Shafi'i, the kitaba is invalidated, and he dies as a slave, and what he leaves belongs to his master. And their proof in this is the saying of Zaid ibn Thabit: 'The mukatab is a slave as long as a dirham remains upon him, he does not inherit nor is he inherited,' narrated by al-Tirmidhi. And because the purpose of the kitaba is his freedom, and its establishment is impossible after his death, so it is invalidated. For it is either established after death as a primary matter, or established before or after death as a retroactive matter. The first is impossible due to lack of capacity (mahall); the second due to the loss of the condition, which is performance; the third is impossible because establishment cannot occur in the present and then be retroactive. As in al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'And we say'; its clarification: that the contract of kitaba is a contract of exchange, and it is not invalidated by the death of one of the contracting parties, namely the master, so likewise it is not invalidated by the death of the other, namely the slave. The common element between them is the need to preserve the contract to revive the right; indeed it is more so because his right is stronger than the master's right, such that the contract is binding on the slave's side, and death is more repugnant to ownership than to being owned, so he is considered alive by estimation. As in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the effect of a mukatab's death when he leaves sufficient assets to pay the remaining kitaba. The Hanafi school holds that the contract is not dissolved; rather, the estate pays the instalments, and the mukatab is considered free from the time of payment, so his heirs inherit him. This is based on the opinions of `Ali and Ibn Mas`ud, and on analogy: the kitaba contract is a contract of exchange (mu`awada), and just as the master's death does not invalidate it, neither does the slave's death. The Shafi`i school, citing the hadith of Zaid ibn Thabit, argues that the mukatab remains a slave until every dirham is paid, and death makes payment impossible, so the contract becomes void. The Hanafi response distinguishes the hadith as applying during life, and argues that freedom can be established by legal estimation (tanziil) after death to fulfill the purpose of the contract.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave in a kitaba contract, who is on the path to freedom by paying instalments.
معاوضة (mu'awada): A commutative contract; an exchange of considerations (e.g., money for freedom).
تأويل (ta'wil): Interpretation; here, the Hanafi understanding of the hadith of Zaid as confined to life.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The kitaba is cancelled, and he dies a slave.
B) The kitaba remains valid, his estate pays, and he dies free.
C) The kitaba is cancelled, but his heirs may pay and free him.
D) The property is divided equally between the master and the heirs.

Key Takeaway
A mukatab dies free if his estate can pay the kitaba; the contract survives death by analogy to any exchange contract.

6 min
Kitabah Contract Survives Mukatab's Death if Assets Sufficient
Explaining Hanafi ruling that kitabah is not void upon death with sufficient assets

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the kitabah contract upon the mukatab's death and explain the qiyas used to justify it.

Core Idea
A mukatab who dies with sufficient assets is deemed free; his kitabah is not void but fulfilled from his estate.

What happens to a slave's freedom contract when he dies before completing payment? The Hanafi school holds that the contract survives if he leaves enough property to cover the remaining dues. This ruling differs from the Shafi'i school and is based on a careful qiyas that treats the kitabah as a contract of exchange. In this lesson, we will dissect the Hanafi position, the opposing evidence, and the legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: if a mukatab dies with assets sufficient to pay the remaining kitabah (al-wafa'), the contract is not void. The mukatab is deemed free posthumously, the payment is taken from his estate, and his heirs inherit the surplus.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and their proof
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the contract becomes void and the mukatab dies a slave. Their evidence includes the hadith 'The mukatab is a slave as long as a dirham of his kitabah remains' and the argument that the purpose (manumission) is impossible after death.
The hadith is also recorded in al-Tirmidhi and other collections.
3
Analyze the Hanafi qiyas
The Hanafi school uses qiyas: the kitabah is a contract of exchange (عقد معاوضة). Since the master's death does not void the contract, the slave's death likewise does not. The common illa is the need to preserve the contract to keep the right alive. The slave's right is even stronger because the contract is binding on him.
The Hanafi response also reinterprets the hadith as pertaining only to inheritance rules, not to the validity of the contract.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under the Hanafi view, the mukatab's heirs inherit any surplus after paying the kitabah. Under the Shafi'i view, the master takes all property. This difference affects family inheritance and the slave's posthumous status.
Children born during or purchased during the kitabah are also freed under the Hanafi ruling.

Book of the Mukatab: His Transactions, Incapacity, and Rulings
If he dies leaving sufficient (assets) to pay, the contract is not void.
'If he dies leaving sufficient': meaning if he leaves property that covers the kitabah payment, (the contract is not void). This is our (Hanafi) position. According to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, the kitabah is void due to the loss of the subject matter. And we say:
[1] His statement: 'If he dies leaving sufficient' means if the mukatab dies leaving property that covers the kitabah payment, his kitabah is not void. This is our position, and it is the view of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud, and we adopt it. According to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, the kitabah becomes void, and he dies as a slave, and what he leaves goes to his master. His Imam in this is Zayd ibn Thabit, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: 'The mukatab is a slave as long as a dirham of his kitabah remains; he does not inherit nor is he inherited from.' Recorded by al-Tirmidhi. And because the purpose of kitabah is his manumission, and establishing it after death is impossible, so it becomes void. This is because it is either established after death as a purpose, or before death or after death based on some connection. The first is impossible due to lack of capacity; the second due to lack of the condition, which is payment; the third due to the impossibility of establishment at the present moment, while something is established in the present moment and then connected. So stated in al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'And we say' — clarification: The contract of kitabah is a contract of exchange. It is not voided by the death of one of the contracting parties, namely the master. So similarly, it is not voided by the death of the other, namely the slave. The common link between them is the need to preserve the contract to keep the right alive. Rather, it is more emphatic because his right is stronger than the master's right, to the extent that the contract is binding on the slave, and death is more suited [to end] ownership than to end owned-ness, so he is treated as alive by estimation. So stated in al-Hidayah.
(1) See: al-Nukat (p.741), etc. (2) Without the phrase 'he does not inherit nor is he inherited from' in Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq (8:405), and in al-Muwatta (2:787), and Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba (4:317) from Ibn 'Umar, and from 'Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather: 'The mukatab is a slave as long as a dirham of his kitabah remains' in Sunan Abi Dawud (2:414), and al-Sunan al-Saghir (9:199), and Ma'rifat al-Sunan (16:203), etc.
(3) al-Hidayah (p.269). (4) al-Hidayah (3:268).

Good insight for the end of the commentary of al-Wiqayah:
And the payment is taken from his property, and it is ruled that he died free, and inheritance is taken from him, and his sons are freed if they were born during his kitabah, or if he purchased them, or if he and his son were jointly made mukatab in one contract, whether the son is young or old.
[0] [{]- He is alive in some rulings" — so likewise here; due to his need to remove the effect of disbelief, which is slavery, or freedom is attributed to before death. (And the payment is taken from his property, and it is ruled that he died free, and inheritance from him, and freeing of his sons if they were born during his kitabah, even if they were born before the kitabah they do not follow him, or if he purchased them, or if he and his son were jointly made mukatab young or old in one contract). [1] His statement: 'in some rulings' means regarding the remaining of his estate under the ruling of his ownership in case there is a debt encompassing it, and regarding preparation and shrouding, and the restriction of bequest to one third, thus in Nata'ij al-Afkar. [2] His statement: 'or freedom is attributed... etc.' This is a second proof: clarification: or freedom is attributed by attributing the cause of payment to before death: i.e., the last part of his life. If it is said: this requires the conditioned preceding the condition, the answer is: that the payment of his deputy is like his payment, so that does not follow, and one should not imagine that freedom precedes payment; rather, payment is estimated before freedom. Thus in al-'Inayah. [3] His statement: 'and inheritance from him' means it is ruled that what remains after the kitabah payment is inheritance from him for his heirs. [4] His statement: 'and freeing... etc.' means his children born during the kitabah are freed. [5] His statement: 'or if he purchased them' means his purchased children during the kitabah are freed. [6] His statement: 'or if he and his son were jointly made mukatab... etc.' means if the father dies leaving sufficient, his son inherits from him if they were jointly mukatab in one contract. We restricted it by saying 'in one contract' to exclude the case where... (1) That the mukatab is freed in the last part of his life; because the kitabah payment is the cause of freedom existing before death, so payment is attributed to before it, making his deputy's payment like his payment, and the rest is in Majma' al-Anhur (2:421). (2) Nata'ij al-Afkar (8:146). (3) al-'Inayah (8:145).
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the fate of a mukatab's kitabah contract upon his death. The Hanafi ruling, attributed to 'Ali and Ibn Mas'ud, is that if the mukatab dies while owning property sufficient to pay the remaining kitabah (الوفاء), the contract is not void. Instead, the mukatab is deemed to have died free, his estate is used to pay the kitabah, and the surplus is inherited by his heirs. His children born during or purchased during the kitabah are also freed. The Shafi'i position, supported by Zayd ibn Thabit, holds that the contract becomes void because the purpose (manumission) cannot be achieved after death, and the mukatab dies as a slave with his property going to the master. The Hanafi response is twofold. First, they argue by qiyas (analogy): the kitabah is a contract of exchange (عقد معاوضة), and just as the master's death does not void it, the slave's death also does not. The common illa is the need to preserve the contract to uphold the rights of both parties, and the slave's right is even stronger because the contract is binding on him. Second, they address the hadith cited by the Shafi'i ("المكاتب عبد ما بقي عليه درهم") by interpreting it as exclusively about inheritance rules, not about the validity of the contract. Additionally, the text explains that the mukatab is treated as alive in certain rulings (like for debt and shrouding) and that freedom is attributed to the last moment of his life, with payment estimated before freedom. This resolves the apparent paradox of achieving freedom after death. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the Hanafi one, as recorded in al-Hidayah and other core texts.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract (kitabah) with his master to earn his freedom by paying a specified sum.
الكتابة (al-kitabah): The contract between a slave and his master whereby the slave will be freed upon payment of a specified amount.
البدل (al-badal): The payment (money or property) agreed upon in the kitabah contract.
الوفاء (al-wafa'): Sufficient assets to cover the full remaining kitabah payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a mukatab dies while owning property sufficient to pay the remaining kitabah, what is the ruling?
A) The contract becomes void, and he dies a slave.
B) He dies a slave, but his master must free him posthumously.
C) He is considered free, his estate pays the kitabah, and the remainder goes to his heirs.
D) His kitabah is canceled, and his property goes to the master.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a mukatab's kitabah contract survives his death if he leaves sufficient assets, treating it as a binding financial obligation that continues posthumously.

Legal Consequences of a Mukatab's Death After Payment: Freedom, Inheritance, and Children
States the legal consequences of a mukatab's death after fulfilling the kitabah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal effects of a mukatab dying after payment and explain the Hanafi rule of istinad (attribution) and its exceptions.

Core Idea
The mukatab who pays his kitabah before death dies free, and his freedom backdates to before death through istihsan.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah clarifies the rulings when a mukatab (a slave under a manumission contract) passes away after having fulfilled his payment. The text establishes that the payment is taken from his estate, he is deemed to have died free, his surplus wealth is inherited, and his children are freed under specific conditions. The author uses the principle of istinad (attribution) based on istihsan to backdate the freedom to just before death, enabling these legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Main Ruling
Recognize that the text addresses a mukatab who dies after paying the full kitabah. The primary ruling: the payment is taken from his estate, he dies free, his heirs inherit the surplus, and his children are freed in certain cases.
The payment is considered made by the mukatab even if it was done by a deputy (e.g., his agent), as the rule of istinad deems it as his own payment.
2
Understand the Key Principle of Istinad
Istinad means attributing the cause (payment) to the time before death, so the effect (freedom) is considered to have occurred just before death. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to benefit the mukatab's heirs and children.
A potential objection is that this would make the effect precede the cause; the text responds by treating the deputy's payment as equivalent to the mukatab's own, so no temporal contradiction arises.
3
Apply the Ruling to the Children's Cases
Children born during the kitabah are set free because they were conceived during the contract. Similarly, children purchased by the mukatab during the kitabah become free upon his death after payment.
The freedom of children is contingent on the father's freedom being backdated; otherwise they would not be free.
4
Distinguish Joint Kitabah with Son
If the father and son were jointly contracted in one kitabah (one contract), the son inherits from the father if the father leaves payment. But if they are in separate kitabah contracts, the son does not inherit because his own freedom is not backdated.
The condition 'one contract' is crucial: only then is the son considered part of the same cause and inherits.
5
Recognize the Evidence (Istihsan)
The proof for the backdating is istihsan, which allows departing from strict analogy (qiyas) to avoid hardship. Here, it prevents the master from inheriting the surplus and ensures the mukatab's heirs are not deprived.
Other schools may reject istihsan and require that the mukatab be actually free at the moment of death, leading to different outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقضي البدل من ماله ، وحكم بموته حراً، والإرث منه ، وعتق بنيه إن ولدوا في كتابته أو شراهم، أو كوتب هو وابنه صغيراً أو كبيراً بمرة) [1] قوله : في بعض الأحكام؛ أي في حق بقاء التركة على حكم ملكه فيما إذا كان عليه دين مستغرق، وفي حق التجهيز والتكفين وتتقيد الوصية في الثلث، هكذا في نتائج الأفكار. [٢] قوله : أو يستند الحرية...الخ ؛ هذا دليل ثان توضيحه أو يستند الحرية باستناد سبب الأداء إلى ما قبل الموت : أي آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته ، فإن قيل : يلزم تقدّم المشروط على الشرط. أجيب عنه : بأن يكون أداء خلفه كأدائه، فلا يلزم ذلك، ولا يتوهم أن العتق يتقدم على الأداء، بل يقدّر الأداء قبل العتق. كذا في العناية. [٣] قوله : والإرث منه؛ أي حكم بأن ما فضل من بدل الكتابة فهو ميراث منه لورثته. [٤] قوله : وعتق ...الخ؛ أي يعتق أولاده ولدوا في حال الكتابة. [0] قوله : أو شراهم؛ أي يعتق أولاده المشترون في حالة الكتابة. [٦] قوله : أو كوتب هو وابنه....الخ؛ أي لو مات الأب وترك الوفاء، ورثه ابنه إن كان هو وابنه مكاتبين بكتابة واحدة، إنما قيدناه بقولنا : بكتابة واحدة احترازاً عما لو كانا مكاتبين كل منهما بعقد واحد؛ لأن الولد إن كان منفرداً بكتابته وأدّاها بعد الموت لم يرثه؛ لأنه مقصود بالكتابة. وإنما يعتق من وقت أداء البدل مقصوراً عليه؛ لأن الاستناد للضرورة، ولا ضرورة في حقه هاهنا، فإذا لم يستند عتقه كان عبداً عند موت أبيه؛ فلهذا لا يرثه. كذا في الكفاية. [1] قوله : كشخص واحد؛ فإذا حكم بحرية الأب يحكم بحريته في تلك الحالة على ما مر. كذا في الهداية.
And the payment is decreed from his wealth, and it is judged that he died free, and inheritance from him, and the freedom of his sons if they were born during his kitabah, or if he bought them, or if he and his son were jointly contracted as a kitabah, whether the son was young or old in one contract.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling for a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission) who dies after having fulfilled the payment. The text states that the payment is taken from his estate, he is deemed to have died free, the surplus of the payment is inherited by his heirs, and his children are set free under certain conditions. The commentary then explains each part: the payment is from his wealth, the freedom is attributed to the moment before death by analogy (istihsan) because his deputy's payment is considered his own; the children born during the kitabah are free; purchased children during the kitabah are also free; and if he and his son were jointly contracted in one kitabah, the son inherits if the father left payment, otherwise the son continues to work and the father becomes free upon payment. The condition 'in one contract' is crucial: if each had a separate kitabah, the son does not inherit because his freedom is not backdated. The proof (istihsan) is that the cause of freedom (payment) is attributed to before death, so the mukatab is free at death and the surplus is inherited.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah with his master to earn his freedom by paying a specified sum.
استناد (istinād): Attribution of the cause (the payment) to the time before death, so that the effect (freedom) is considered to have occurred at that earlier time.
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitābah): The payment (usually in installments) that the mukatab agrees to pay to his master in exchange for his freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the status of a child born to a mukatab during the kitabah if the mukatab pays the full kitabah and dies?
A) The child remains a slave because the father's freedom is not backdated.
B) The child is free because the father's freedom is backdated to before death, and the child was born during the kitabah.
C) The child is free only if the father purchased him after the kitabah.
D) The child must pay the remaining kitabah to become free.

Key Takeaway
When a mukatab pays the kitabah and dies, his freedom is backdated to before death by istihsan, and all related rulings (inheritance, children's freedom) take effect.

Dispute on the Purchased Child When Mukatab Dies Without Payment
Explains the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the purchased child's obligation when the mukatab dies without payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the purchased child's obligation when the mukatab dies without payment and identify which position is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A purchased child is not automatically covered by the mukatab's contract; he either pays in full or continues installments.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a child purchased by a mukatab (a slave contracted for freedom). When the mukatab dies without leaving sufficient payment, the legal status of this purchased child differs according to the founding imams. Understanding this ikhtilaf and its resolution is key to applying the correct ruling in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State Abu Hanifa's view and the Sahibayn's view clearly.
Abu Hanifa gives an option; Sahibayn impose an obligation.
2
Locate the legal reasoning behind each position
Identify the dalil and ta'lil for each view.
Abu Hanifa focuses on the contract condition (ajal), Sahibayn use qiyas to the born child.
3
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence of each ruling for the child?
One gives a choice, the other imposes a duty.
4
Identify the mufta bihi position
Which view is relied upon in fatwa and why.
The Sahibayn's view is mufta bihi according to later authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يترك وفاء فمن ولد في كتابته سعى على نجومه، وإذا أدى حكم بعتق أبيه قبل موته ويعتقه، ومن شراه أدى البدل حالاً، أو رد رقيقاً) ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما الولد المشترى يسعى على نجوم الأب أيضاً، لأنه كوتب بتبعية الأب. [1] قوله : حكم بعتق أبيه...الخ؛ لأن الولد داخل في كتابته، وكسبه ككسبه، فيخلفه في الأداء، وصار كما إذا ترك وفاء. كذا في الهداية. [٣] قوله : ومن شراه...الخ؛ أي إن ترك ولداً مشترى في الكتابة، فله الخيار بين أن يؤدي بدل الكتابة حالاً، وبين أن يردّ رقيقاً. [٤] قوله : هذا عند أبي حنيفة له، وله الفرق بين الفصلين أن الأجل يثبت شرطاً في العقد، فيثبت في حق من دخل تحت العقد، والمشترى لم يدخل؛ لأنه لم يضف إليه (الكفاية) (۸: ١٤٦ - ١٤٧).
And if he did not leave payment, then whoever was born during his kitabah strives for his installments, and when he pays, it is judged that his father was free before his death, and he is set free. And whoever he purchased pays the payment immediately, or returns as a slave. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two, the purchased child strives on the installments of the father as well, because he was contracted by following the father.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a purchased child (a slave bought by the mukatab during the kitabah) when the mukatab dies without leaving sufficient payment. Abu Hanifa holds that such a purchased child has a choice: either pay the entire kitabah payment immediately (and become free) or be returned to the master as a slave. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the purchased child should continue to work and pay the installments just like a child born during the kitabah. The reasoning given is that the purchased child was not originally included in the kitabah contract, so the deferment (ajal) that was stipulated in the contract does not apply to him; thus he must either pay in full or be returned. The disciples argue that since the child was purchased by the mukatab and is effectively following the father, he should be treated similarly. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is the view of the two disciples, as it is more practical and consistent with the principle of following the father's contract.

Key Terms
ولد مشترى (walad mushtarā): A slave purchased by the mukatab during the period of his kitabah.
سعى على نجومه (sa'ā 'alā nujūmihi): To strive to earn and pay the installments (nujum) of the kitabah.
أجل (ajal): Deferment; the period allowed for paying installments in the kitabah contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, what happens to a child purchased by a mukatab when the mukatab dies without leaving payment?
A) The child is immediately freed.
B) The child may choose to pay the entire kitabah or be returned as a slave.
C) The child must continue to pay the installments of the father's kitabah.
D) The child is automatically returned to the master as a slave.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon ruling for a purchased child of a deceased mukatab is that he must continue to pay the installments, following the two disciples' view.
